(۲۲) قوله « تالياً»: لتلوه الجزء الاول. (۲۳) قوله « والموضوع»: هذا تقسيم (التقسيم خ ل للقضية الحملية باعتبار الموضوع ) و لهذا لوحظ في تسمية الاقسام حال الموضوع فيسمى ما هو موضوعه شخص شخصية و على هذا القياس. و محصل التقسيم : ان الموضوع أما جزئى حقيقى كقولنا : «هذا انسان» (۲۴) او كلى، وعلى الثانى (۲۵) فاما ان يكون الحكم على نفس حقيقة هذا الكلى او على افراده و على الثانى فاما ان يبين كمية الأفراد المحكوم عليها بان يبين ان الحكم على كلها أو على بعضها او لايبين ذلك بل يهمل ، فالاولى شخصية والثانية طبيعية (۲۶) والثالثة محصورة والرابعة مهملة (٢٧). ثم ان المحصورة ان بين فيها ان الحكم على كل افراد الموضوع فكلية و ان بين ان الحكم على بعض افراده فجزئية و كل منهما اما موجبة أو سالبة ولابد في كل من تلك المحصورات الاربع من أمريبين كمية افراد الموضوع يسمى ذلك الامر بـ «السور» اذ كما سور البلد محيط به، كذلك هذا الامر محيط بما حكم عليه من افراد الموضوع، فسور الموجبة الكليه هو «كل» (۲۸) ولام الاستغراق (۲۹) وما يفيد معناهما من أى لغة كانت (۳۰) و سور الموجبة الجزئية هو «بعض» (۳۱) و «واحد» و ما يفيد مؤداهما (۳۲) و سور السالبة الكلية لاشىء و لا)) (واحد) و نظائرهما، (۳۳) و سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض » و « بعض ليس » و « ليس كل» (٣٤) و ما يساويها . قوله وتلازم الجزئية»: اعلم ان القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم هي المحصورات (٥) قول المصنف كمية افراده...» بتشديد الميم والياء اصلها كم بسكون الميم، فالحق به التاء المصدرية وشدد الميم حتى يكون ثلاثياً اذلم يوجد ثنائى عومل به تلك المعاملة الا «الهوية». و اما الياء فهي مقحمة بين الميم والتاء فرقاً بين المصدر الوصفى والقياسي و تشديدها للتشبيه بالياء التي اتى للنسبة هكذا رأينا فى بعض المؤلفات شيخ عبد الرحيم)
In the divisions of the proposition: 57 first part is called 'antecedent' and the second 'consequent'. The subject: if it is determinate, the proposition is called 'particular' (shakhsiyya) and 'specific' (makhsusa); if it is the essence itself, then 'natural' (tabi'iyya); and if it is between the quantity of its individuals, either all or some, then 'quantified' (mahsura), either universal or particular; and that by which it is clarified is the quantifier; otherwise it is 'neglected' (muhmala), and the particular is implied. (22) His statement 'antecedent': because it precedes in mentioning. (23) His statement 'consequent': because it follows the first part. His statement 'and the subject': this is a division (the division is for the categorical proposition in consideration of the subject). For this reason, the names are observed according to the state of the subject: what is its subject if particular is called particular, and so on. The summary of the division: the subject is either a real particular like our saying 'this human' or a universal. In the second case, either the judgment is on the essence itself of this universal or on its individuals. In the second case, either the quantity of the individuals judged is clarified by clarifying that the judgment is on all of them or on some of them, or it is not clarified but neglected. So the first is particular, the second natural, the third quantified, and the fourth neglected. Then the quantified: if it is clarified in it that the judgment is on all individuals of the subject, then universal; and if it is clarified that the judgment is on some of its individuals, then particular. Each of these is either affirmative or negative. And in each of those four quantified propositions there must be two things: the quantity of the individuals of the subject. That thing is called the 'quantifier' (sur), for just as the wall of a city encompasses it, so this thing encompasses what is judged of the individuals of the subject. The quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'kull' (all) and the lām of inclusiveness and whatever conveys their meaning from any language. The quantifier of the particular affirmative is 'ba'd' (some) and 'wāḥid' (one) and whatever conveys their meaning. The quantifier of the universal negative is 'lā shay' (nothing) and 'lā wāḥid' (not one) and their like. The quantifier of the particular negative is 'laysa ba'd' (not some) and 'ba'd laysa' (some not) and 'laysa kull' (not all) and their equivalents. His statement 'and the particular is implied': Know that the propositions considered in the sciences are the quantified ones. (5) The author's statement 'quantity of its individuals...' with shaddah on the mīm and yā; its origin is 'kamm' with sukun on the mīm, then the tā of the verbal noun was attached and the mīm was made shaddah so that it becomes triliteral, since no biliteral is treated that way except 'huwiyyah'. As for the yā, it is inserted between the mīm and tā to distinguish the verbal noun from the regular noun, and its shaddah is for resemblance to the yā of attribution. This is from the works of Shaykh Abd al-Rahim.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage classifies categorical propositions based on the nature of the subject. It first notes the terminology for parts of a conditional (antecedent and consequent), though the focus is on categorical. The commentary explains each term. The subject can be a real particular (juz'ī ḥaqīqī) like 'this human', or a universal. For universal subjects, the judgment may be on the essence itself (tabi'iyya) or on its individuals. If it is on individuals, the quantity may be specified (mahsura) or left unspecified (muhmala). The mahsura is further divided into universal and particular, each affirmative or negative, giving four quantified propositions. Each quantified form requires a quantifier (sur): for universal affirmative 'kull' or the inclusive lām, for particular affirmative 'ba'd' or 'wāḥid', for universal negative 'lā shay' or 'lā wāḥid', for particular negative 'laysa ba'd' or 'ba'd laysa' or 'laysa kull'. The passage concludes that only quantified propositions are considered in the sciences. An example of shakhsiyya: 'Zayd is standing.' Tabi'iyya: 'Man is rational.' Mahsura: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Muhmala: 'Man is standing' (without specifying which man). Universal affirmative: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Particular negative: 'Not every book is useful.'

Four Types of Categorical Propositions by Subject
Author explains the fourfold division of categorical propositions by subject type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into personal, natural, quantified, or indeterminate based on its subject.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions divide into four types by how the subject refers to reality.

In this lesson, we examine the author's classification of categorical propositions according to the nature of the subject. This division is fundamental for understanding how propositions function in Islamic logic, as each type has distinct logical properties. We will learn to identify each type and see why only quantified propositions are ultimately used in the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of division
The division is based on the subject (الموضوع) of the categorical proposition. The author considers whether the subject is a concrete particular, a universal essence, or a universal with specified or unspecified quantity.
The predicate is not involved in this division.
2
Identify the four types
The four types are: personal (شخصية) where the subject is a concrete individual (e.g., 'this man'); natural (طبيعية) where the subject is the essence itself (e.g., 'man' as a nature); quantified (محصورة) where the quantity of the subject is specified; and indeterminate (مهملة) where the quantity is left unspecified.
Quantified is further divided into universal and particular.
3
Apply to sample propositions
Take the proposition 'Every Muslim must pray'. The subject is 'every Muslim', which is universal quantified. 'Some scholars are generous' is particular quantified. 'Zaid is standing' is personal. 'Man is an animal' (as a natural kind) is natural if taken as essence, but if taken as individuals it would be quantified.
Context determines the classification.
4
Check for completeness
The author's classification is exhaustive: any subject is either a particular or a universal; if universal, the judgment is on the essence or on individuals; if on individuals, quantity is specified or not. This covers all possibilities.
This is a conceptual division, not a list of existing propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القضية. مقدماً والثانى تالياً والموضوع ان كان مشخصاً سميت القضية شخصية و مخصوصة و ان كان نفس الحقيقة فطبيعية و ما يبين كمية افراده كلاً او بعضاً فمحصورة كلية او جزئية و ما به البيان سور و الا فمهملة وتلازم الجزئية. قوله «مقدماً»: لتقدمه في الذكر. قوله « تالياً»: لتلوه الجزء الاول. قوله « والموضوع»: هذا تقسيم للقضية الحملية باعتبار الموضوع و لهذا لوحظ في تسمية الاقسام حال الموضوع فيسمى ما هو موضوعه شخص شخصية و على هذا القياس. و محصل التقسيم : ان الموضوع اما جزئى حقيقى كقولنا : «هذا انسان» او كلى، وعلى الثانى فاما ان يكون الحكم على نفس حقيقة هذا الكلى او على افراده و على الثانى فاما ان يبين كمية الأفراد المحكوم عليها بان يبين ان الحكم على كلها أو على بعضها او لايبين ذلك بل يهمل ، فالاولى شخصية والثانية طبيعية والثالثة محصورة والرابعة مهملة. ثم ان المحصورة ان بين فيها ان الحكم على كل افراد الموضوع فكلية و ان بين ان الحكم على بعض افراده فجزئية و كل منهما اما موجبة أو سالبة ولابد في كل من تلك المحصورات الاربع من أمريبين كمية افراد الموضوع يسمى ذلك الامر بـ «السور» اذ كما سور البلد محيط به، كذلك هذا الامر محيط بما حكم عليه من افراد الموضوع، فسور الموجبة الكليه هو «كل» ولام الاستغراق وما يفيد معناهما من أى لغة كانت و سور الموجبة الجزئية هو «بعض» و «واحد» و ما يفيد مؤداهما و سور السالبة الكلية لاشىء و لا واحد و نظائرهما، و سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض و بعض ليس و ليس كل و ما يساويها . قوله وتلازم الجزئية: اعلم ان القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم هي المحصورات الاربع لاغير و ذلك لان المهملة والجزئية متلازمتان اذ كلما صدق الحكم على افراد الموضوع فى الجملة صدق على بعض افراده و بالعكس فالمهملة مندرجة تحت الجزئية، و الشخصية لا يبحث عنها بخصوصها فانه لا كمال في معرفة الجزئيات لتغيرها وعدم ثباتها بل انما يبحث عنها فى ضمن المصورات التي يحكم فيها على الاشخاص اجمالاً والطبيعية لا يبحث عنها فى العلوم اصلا فان الطبايع الكلية من حيث نفس مفهومها كما هو مفهومها كما هو موضوع الطبيعية لامن حيث تحققها فى ضمن الاشخاص غير موجودة فى الخارج فلا كمال في معرفة احوالها فانحصر القضاياء المعتبرة فى المحصورات الاربع.
On the divisions of the proposition. The first part is the antecedent and the second is the consequent. And the subject: if it is a particular, the proposition is called personal and specific; if it is the essence itself, it is natural; and what clarifies the quantity of its individuals, either all or some, then it is quantified, universal or particular; and what clarifies it is the quantifier; otherwise it is indeterminate, and it is equivalent to the particular. His statement 'antecedent': because it precedes in mention. His statement 'consequent': because it follows the first part. His statement 'and the subject': this is a division of the categorical proposition with respect to the subject, and therefore the state of the subject is considered in naming the divisions, so what has a particular subject is called personal, and so on. The summary of the division: the subject is either a true particular, like our saying 'this man', or a universal. On the second [case], either the judgment is on the essence of this universal itself or on its individuals; on the second, either the quantity of the individuals judged is clarified by stating that the judgment is on all of them or on some of them, or it is not clarified but left indeterminate. The first is personal, the second natural, the third quantified, and the fourth indeterminate. Then, the quantified: if it is clarified that the judgment is on all individuals of the subject, it is universal; if it is clarified that the judgment is on some of its individuals, it is particular; and each of them is either affirmative or negative. And in each of those four quantified [propositions], there must be two things: the quantity of the individuals of the subject, and that thing is called the 'quantifier', because just as the wall of a city surrounds it, so this thing surrounds what is judged of the individuals of the subject. The quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'every' and the lam of inclusiveness and whatever conveys their meaning in any language. The quantifier of the particular affirmative is 'some' and 'one' and whatever conveys their meaning. The quantifier of the universal negative is 'nothing' and 'no one' and their equivalents. The quantifier of the particular negative is 'not some' and 'some are not' and 'not every' and what equals them. His statement 'and it is equivalent to the particular': Know that the propositions considered in the sciences are the four quantified only, because the indeterminate and the particular are equivalent, for whenever a judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general, it is true of some of its individuals, and vice versa; thus the indeterminate is included under the particular. And the personal is not investigated specifically, because there is no perfection in knowing particulars due to their change and lack of stability; rather it is investigated within the quantified [propositions] that make judgments on individuals in aggregate. And the natural is not investigated in the sciences at all, for the universal natures in terms of their concepts themselves, as they are the subject of natural science, not in terms of their realization within individuals, do not exist in the external world, so there is no perfection in knowing their states. Thus the propositions considered are confined to the four quantified.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and classification of categorical propositions from a hashiya on a standard logic text. The author begins by identifying the parts of a proposition: the antecedent (مقدّم) and consequent (تالي), which are commonly called subject and predicate. He then focuses on the subject and divides propositions based on the nature of the subject. If the subject is a concrete particular (e.g., 'this man'), the proposition is personal (شخصية). If the subject is the essence itself (e.g., 'man' considered as a nature), it is natural (طبيعية). If the subject's quantity (all or some) is specified, it is quantified (محصورة). If not specified, it is indeterminate (مهملة). The author then explains that quantified propositions are further divided into universal and particular, each with affirmative and negative forms. Each of these four has a quantifier (سور) that indicates the scope. The quantifiers are listed: 'كل' for universal affirmative, 'بعض' for particular affirmative, etc. Finally, the author argues that only the four quantified propositions are used in sciences because: (1) the indeterminate is equivalent to the particular (if a judgment applies to the individuals in general, it applies to some), (2) personal propositions are not investigated due to their particularity and change, (3) natural propositions are not investigated because universal natures as such do not exist externally. Throughout, the author follows the matn's structure, expanding each point with explanation and justification.

طبيعية
محصورة
مهملة

Propositions

The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Author lists the quantifiers for each of the four quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four quantified propositions (A, E, I, O) and state their corresponding quantifiers in Arabic.

Core Idea
Each of the four quantified propositions has a specific quantifier that determines its logical force.

This lesson focuses on the quantified propositions (محصورة) and their quantifiers (سور). The author lists the quantifiers for each of the four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Knowing these quantifiers is essential for translating natural language statements into precise logical forms.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two divisions
Quantified propositions are first divided by quantity (universal vs particular) and then by quality (affirmative vs negative), yielding four types.
These are the standard A (كل), E (لا شيء), I (بعض), O (ليس بعض) propositions.
2
Learn the quantifiers for each
Universal affirmative: 'كل' (kull) or 'الاستغراق' (the lam of inclusiveness). Universal negative: 'لا شيء' (la shay') or 'لا واحد' (la wahid). Particular affirmative: 'بعض' (ba'd) or 'واحد' (wahid). Particular negative: 'ليس بعض' (laysa ba'd), 'بعض ليس' (ba'dun laysa), 'ليس كل' (laysa kull).
The quantifier 'sur' surrounds the subject like a city wall (سور البلد).
3
Match examples to quantifiers
For A: 'Every [كل] Muslim is required to pray.' For E: 'No [لا شيء] disbeliever will enter Paradise.' For I: 'Some [بعض] scholars are wealthy.' For O: 'Not every [ليس كل] rich person is generous.'
Examples must be from Islamic sources.
4
Verify with the source
Check the Arabic text: 'فسور الموجبة الكليه هو «كل» ولام الاستغراق... سور الموجبة الجزئية هو «بعض» و «واحد»... سور السالبة الكلية لاشىء و لا واحد... سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض و بعض ليس و ليس كل...'
Memorize these quantifiers — they appear in syllogistic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Propositions

The Indeterminate and Particular Are Equivalent
Author explains the equivalence between indeterminate and particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the indeterminate proposition (مهملة) is logically equivalent to the particular proposition (جزئية).

Core Idea
An indeterminate proposition is treated as particular because both are true under the same conditions.

The author states that the indeterminate proposition (مهملة) is equivalent to (تلازم) the particular proposition (جزئية). This is a key principle in Islamic logic: when a proposition does not specify quantity, it is assumed to be particular. Understanding this equivalence simplifies logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the indeterminate proposition
An indeterminate proposition (مهملة) is one where the quantity of the subject is not specified. For example, 'Alimun qā'imun' (A scholar is standing) without 'every' or 'some'.
In Arabic, the indefinite noun (نكرة) often indicates indeterminacy.
2
Recall the particular proposition
A particular proposition (جزئية) explicitly states that the judgment applies to some (بعض) individuals of the subject. Example: 'Some scholars are generous.'
Particular is always affirmative or negative with quantifier 'some' or equivalent.
3
Grasp the equivalence
The author argues that whenever a judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general (i.e., without specifying quantity), it is true of some of its individuals. Conversely, if it is true of some individuals, it is true of them in general (without specifying all). Thus, the indeterminate is logically equivalent to the particular.
This holds only if the subject has existing individuals (otherwise the proposition may be false).
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider the statement 'The debtor must repay the debt' without a quantifier. This is indeterminate, but in usul al-fiqh it is interpreted as a particular ruling applying to some debtors (unless context indicates universality). This equivalence allows jurists to treat ambiguous statements as particular unless evidence specifies otherwise.
The equivalence is a default rule in reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القضية. مقدماً والثانى تالياً والموضوع ان كان مشخصاً سميت القضية شخصية و مخصوصة و ان كان نفس الحقيقة فطبيعية و ما يبين كمية افراده كلاً او بعضاً فمحصورة كلية او جزئية و ما به البيان سور و الا فمهملة وتلازم الجزئية. قوله «مقدماً»: لتقدمه في الذكر. قوله « تالياً»: لتلوه الجزء الاول. قوله « والموضوع»: هذا تقسيم للقضية الحملية باعتبار الموضوع و لهذا لوحظ في تسمية الاقسام حال الموضوع فيسمى ما هو موضوعه شخص شخصية و على هذا القياس. و محصل التقسيم : ان الموضوع اما جزئى حقيقى كقولنا : «هذا انسان» او كلى، وعلى الثانى فاما ان يكون الحكم على نفس حقيقة هذا الكلى او على افراده و على الثانى فاما ان يبين كمية الأفراد المحكوم عليها بان يبين ان الحكم على كلها أو على بعضها او لايبين ذلك بل يهمل ، فالاولى شخصية والثانية طبيعية والثالثة محصورة والرابعة مهملة. ثم ان المحصورة ان بين فيها ان الحكم على كل افراد الموضوع فكلية و ان بين ان الحكم على بعض افراده فجزئية و كل منهما اما موجبة أو سالبة ولابد في كل من تلك المحصورات الاربع من أمريبين كمية افراد الموضوع يسمى ذلك الامر بـ «السور» اذ كما سور البلد محيط به، كذلك هذا الامر محيط بما حكم عليه من افراد الموضوع، فسور الموجبة الكليه هو «كل» ولام الاستغراق وما يفيد معناهما من أى لغة كانت و سور الموجبة الجزئية هو «بعض» و «واحد» و ما يفيد مؤداهما و سور السالبة الكلية لاشىء و لا واحد و نظائرهما، و سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض و بعض ليس و ليس كل و ما يساويها . قوله وتلازم الجزئية: اعلم ان القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم هي المحصورات الاربع لاغير و ذلك لان المهملة والجزئية متلازمتان اذ كلما صدق الحكم على افراد الموضوع فى الجملة صدق على بعض افراده و بالعكس فالمهملة مندرجة تحت الجزئية، و الشخصية لا يبحث عنها بخصوصها فانه لا كمال في معرفة الجزئيات لتغيرها وعدم ثباتها بل انما يبحث عنها فى ضمن المصورات التي يحكم فيها على الاشخاص اجمالاً والطبيعية لا يبحث عنها فى العلوم اصلا فان الطبايع الكلية من حيث نفس مفهومها كما هو مفهومها كما هو موضوع الطبيعية لامن حيث تحققها فى ضمن الاشخاص غير موجودة فى الخارج فلا كمال في معرفة احوالها فانحصر القضاياء المعتبرة فى المحصورات الاربع.
On the divisions of the proposition. The first part is the antecedent and the second is the consequent. And the subject: if it is a particular, the proposition is called personal and specific; if it is the essence itself, it is natural; and what clarifies the quantity of its individuals, either all or some, then it is quantified, universal or particular; and what clarifies it is the quantifier; otherwise it is indeterminate, and it is equivalent to the particular. His statement 'antecedent': because it precedes in mention. His statement 'consequent': because it follows the first part. His statement 'and the subject': this is a division of the categorical proposition with respect to the subject, and therefore the state of the subject is considered in naming the divisions, so what has a particular subject is called personal, and so on. The summary of the division: the subject is either a true particular, like our saying 'this man', or a universal. On the second [case], either the judgment is on the essence of this universal itself or on its individuals; on the second, either the quantity of the individuals judged is clarified by stating that the judgment is on all of them or on some of them, or it is not clarified but left indeterminate. The first is personal, the second natural, the third quantified, and the fourth indeterminate. Then, the quantified: if it is clarified that the judgment is on all individuals of the subject, it is universal; if it is clarified that the judgment is on some of its individuals, it is particular; and each of them is either affirmative or negative. And in each of those four quantified [propositions], there must be two things: the quantity of the individuals of the subject, and that thing is called the 'quantifier', because just as the wall of a city surrounds it, so this thing surrounds what is judged of the individuals of the subject. The quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'every' and the lam of inclusiveness and whatever conveys their meaning in any language. The quantifier of the particular affirmative is 'some' and 'one' and whatever conveys their meaning. The quantifier of the universal negative is 'nothing' and 'no one' and their equivalents. The quantifier of the particular negative is 'not some' and 'some are not' and 'not every' and what equals them. His statement 'and it is equivalent to the particular': Know that the propositions considered in the sciences are the four quantified only, because the indeterminate and the particular are equivalent, for whenever a judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general, it is true of some of its individuals, and vice versa; thus the indeterminate is included under the particular. And the personal is not investigated specifically, because there is no perfection in knowing particulars due to their change and lack of stability; rather it is investigated within the quantified [propositions] that make judgments on individuals in aggregate. And the natural is not investigated in the sciences at all, for the universal natures in terms of their concepts themselves, as they are the subject of natural science, not in terms of their realization within individuals, do not exist in the external world, so there is no perfection in knowing their states. Thus the propositions considered are confined to the four quantified.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and classification of categorical propositions from a hashiya on a standard logic text. The author begins by identifying the parts of a proposition: the antecedent (مقدّم) and consequent (تالي), which are commonly called subject and predicate. He then focuses on the subject and divides propositions based on the nature of the subject. If the subject is a concrete particular (e.g., 'this man'), the proposition is personal (شخصية). If the subject is the essence itself (e.g., 'man' considered as a nature), it is natural (طبيعية). If the subject's quantity (all or some) is specified, it is quantified (محصورة). If not specified, it is indeterminate (مهملة). The author then explains that quantified propositions are further divided into universal and particular, each with affirmative and negative forms. Each of these four has a quantifier (سور) that indicates the scope. The quantifiers are listed: 'كل' for universal affirmative, 'بعض' for particular affirmative, etc. Finally, the author argues that only the four quantified propositions are used in sciences because: (1) the indeterminate is equivalent to the particular (if a judgment applies to the individuals in general, it applies to some), (2) personal propositions are not investigated due to their particularity and change, (3) natural propositions are not investigated because universal natures as such do not exist externally. Throughout, the author follows the matn's structure, expanding each point with explanation and justification.

جزئية
تلازم

The indeterminate proposition is logically equivalent to the particular proposition, so it is treated as particular in scientific reasoning.

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
Why Only Four Quantified Propositions Are Used in Sciences
Author argues that only the four quantified propositions are used in sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why Islamic logicians restrict scientific propositions to the four quantified types.

Core Idea
Personal, natural, and indeterminate propositions are excluded from sciences for specific reasons, leaving only the four quantified.

After defining the types of categorical propositions, the author argues that only the four quantified propositions are considered in the sciences. He provides three reasons: the equivalence of indeterminate to particular, the instability of personal propositions, and the non-existence of natural universals. This lesson unpacks these reasons and shows why they are essential for scientific rigor.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three excluded types
The three types excluded from sciences are: personal (شخصية), natural (طبيعية), and indeterminate (مهملة). Only quantified (محصورة) remain.
The four quantified are universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
2
Examine the exclusion of indeterminate
The indeterminate is excluded because it is equivalent to the particular, so it is subsumed under the particular quantified. No need for a separate category.
This is a practical simplification.
3
Examine the exclusion of personal
Personal propositions are about particular individuals (e.g., 'Zaid is tall'), which change and are unstable. Sciences seek general knowledge (كلي), so personal propositions are not investigated as such; they are only considered within quantified propositions that make general statements about individuals.
For example, 'Every human is mortal' covers all individuals, not just Zaid.
4
Examine the exclusion of natural
Natural propositions predicate on the essence itself (e.g., 'Man is an animal' as a nature). But universal natures as concepts do not exist externally (فإن الطبايع الكلية من حيث نفس مفهومها... غير موجودة في الخارج). Therefore, there is no perfection in studying their states. Sciences deal with existent things, so natural propositions are excluded.
This is a realist epistemological stance: only quantified propositions about individuals yield reliable knowledge.

The Three Types of Affirmative Propositions Based on Subject Existence
Author explains the necessity of subject existence in affirmative propositions and its three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an affirmative proposition is external, real, or mental based on the mode of subject existence and apply this distinction to Islamic theological discourse.

Core Idea
Not all affirmative propositions require actual external existence of the subject; some allow hypothetical or mental existence.

In logic, the truth of an affirmative proposition depends on the existence of its subject, but that existence can be understood in different ways. This lesson explains the three types of affirmative propositions recognized in Islamic logic: external, real (hypothetical), and mental. Understanding these distinctions is crucial when analyzing statements about possible and impossible entities in theology and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Look at the passage where the author lists the three types: الخارجية (external), الحقيقية (real/hypothetical), and الذهنية (mental). Each is defined by how the subject exists.
The text says 'لابد في الموجبة من وجود الموضوع اما محققا و هى الخارجية او مقدرا فالحقيقية او ذهنا فالذهنية.'
2
Understand the basis of each type
External: subject actually exists in the external world. Real: subject is considered hypothetically as a possible existent. Mental: subject exists only in the mind.
The real type applies only to possible individuals, not impossible ones.
3
Distinguish real from mental
The real type is for subjects that could exist externally (like 'man' hypothetically), while the mental type is for subjects that cannot exist externally (like 'partner of God').
The author explicitly says 'هذا انما اعتبروه في الموضوعات التي ليست لها افراد ممكنة التحقق في الخارج.'
4
Apply to examples from the text
Analyze the examples: 'every man is an animal' can be external or real; 'the partner of God is impossible' is mental.
Consider how the same sentence can be interpreted differently depending on the mode.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولابد في الموجبة من وجود الموضوع اما محققاً و هى الخارجية او مقدراً فالحقيقية او ذهنا فالذهنية. وقد يجعل حرف السلب جزء من جزء الموضوع او المحمول او كليهما. قوله ولابد في «الموجبة» اى فى صدقها وذلك لان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت شيء لشيء و ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت الشيء له اعنى الموضوع فانما يصدق هذا الحكم اذا كان الموضوع محققاً موجوداً أما في الخارج ان كان الحكم بثبوت المحمول له هناك او فى الذهن كذلك ثم القضايا الحملية المعتبرة في العلوم باعتبار وجود موضوعها لها ثلاثة اقسام لان الحكم فيها اما على الموضوع الموجود في الخارج محققاً نحو كل انسان حیوان بمعنى ان كل انسان موجود في الخارج حيوان فى الخارج و اما على الموضوع الموجود فى الخارج مقدراً نحو: «كل انسان حیوان» :بمعنى ان كل ما لو وجد فى الخارج كان انسانا فهو على تقدير وجوده في الخارج حيوان و هذا الموجود المقدر انما اعتبروه في الافراد الممكنة لا الممتنعة كافراد اللاشيء و شریک البارى تعالى و اما على الموضوع الموجود فى الذهن كقولنا: شریک الباری ممتنع بمعنى ان كلما يوجد في العقل ويفرضه العقل شریک البارى فهو موصوف في الذهن بالامتناع في الخارج و هذا انما اعتبروه في الموضوعات التي ليست لها افراد ممكنة التحقق في الخارج. قوله «حرف السلب» كـ «لا» و «ليس و غيرهما مما يشاركهما في معنى السلب. قوله من جزء اما من الموضوع فقط أو من المحمول فقط او من كليهما.
His statement: 'And it is necessary in the affirmative [proposition] that the subject exist' either actually, which is the external [type], or hypothetically, which is the real [type], or mentally, which is the mental [type]. And the particle of negation may be made a part of the part of the subject or the predicate or both. His statement 'and it is necessary' in 'the affirmative' means in its truth, because the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of something for something, and the affirmation of something for something is subsidiary to the existence of the thing for it, meaning the subject. So this judgment is only true if the subject is actually existing: either externally if the judgment is the affirmation of the predicate for it there, or mentally similarly. Then the categorical propositions considered in the sciences, with respect to the existence of their subject, have three divisions: because the judgment is either on the subject existing externally actually, like 'every man is an animal' meaning that every man existing externally is an animal externally; or on the subject existing externally hypothetically, like 'every man is an animal' meaning that every thing that, if it existed externally, would be a man, then on the assumption of its existence externally it is an animal; and this hypothetical existence is only considered for possible individuals, not impossible ones like individuals of 'nothing' and the partner of God most high; or on the subject existing mentally, like our saying 'the partner of God is impossible' meaning that whatever exists in the mind and the mind supposes to be a partner of God, it is described in the mind as impossible in the external world; and this is only considered for subjects that do not have individuals possible to realize externally. His statement 'the particle of negation' like 'la' and 'laysa' and others that share their meaning of negation. His statement 'from a part' either from the subject only or from the predicate only or from both.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the condition that in an affirmative categorical proposition, the subject must exist in some sense. The author begins by quoting the matn: 'It is necessary in the affirmative [proposition] that the subject exist.' He then expands: the subject can exist in three ways: 1) actually in the external world (خارجية), e.g., 'Every man is an animal' meaning actually existing men; 2) hypothetically in the external world (حقيقية), where the subject is a possible existent but not yet actualized, e.g., 'Every man is an animal' interpreted as 'whatever would be a man if it existed' – this applies only to possible individuals, not impossibilities like the partner of God; 3) mentally (ذهنية), where the subject exists only in the mind, e.g., 'The partner of God is impossible' – the subject 'partner of God' exists mentally but not externally. The text then notes that the negation particle (like 'لا' and 'ليس') can be considered part of the subject, predicate, or both, affecting the analysis of negative propositions. This commentary refines the sharh by clarifying the different modes of subject existence and their application in scientific discourse.

Key Terms
الموجبة الخارجية
الموجبة الحقيقية
الموجبة الذهنية
حرف السلب

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions vary in how the subject exists: actually, hypothetically, or mentally, each serving a distinct role in logical and theological analysis.

Three Kinds of Subject Existence in Affirmative Propositions
Yazdi explains three kinds of subject existence and concludes with four categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an affirmative categorical proposition as external, hypothetical, or mental based on the existence of its subject, and explain why only four categorical propositions are considered in logic.

Core Idea
The subject of an affirmative proposition must exist either externally, hypothetically, or mentally; this determines the proposition's truth conditions.

In this passage, Yazdi lays out a foundational classification of affirmative propositions based on how the subject's existence is considered. He distinguishes between external existence (الخارجية), hypothetical existence (الحقيقية), and mental existence (الذهنية). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assessing the truth conditions of propositions in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with theological and metaphysical concepts where subjects may not have external existence. The passage also explains why only the four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are studied in logic, by eliminating singular, natural, and indefinite propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of subject existence
From the passage, extract the three kinds: actual external existence, hypothetical existence (for possible individuals), and mental existence (for impossible or purely mental subjects).
2
Match each type to its Arabic term
Recognize that الخارجية is for actual external existence, الحقيقية for hypothetical, and الذهنية for mental. Note the examples given: 'كل إنسان حيوان' for external and hypothetical (with different interpretations), and 'شريك الباري ممتنع' for mental.
3
Understand the elimination of non-categorical propositions
The passage states that the universal-negative (المهملة) is subsumed under the particular, singular propositions are not studied due to change, and natural propositions are not studied because they have no external existence. Thus only four categoricals remain.
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Given an Islamic science proposition, determine which type of existence its subject has. For instance, in the legal maxim 'كل مسكر حرام', the subject 'مسكر' exists externally (actual intoxicants), so it is an external proposition.

قوله ولابد في الموجبة اي في صدقها وذلك لان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت شيء لشيء و ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع تثبوت التبت له اعني الموضوع فانما يصدق هذا الحكم اذا كان الموضوع محققا موجودا اما في الخارج ان كان الحكم بثبوت المحمول له هناك او في الذهن كذلك ثم القضايا الحملية المعتبرة في العلوم باعتبار وجود موضوعها لها ثلاثة اقسام لان الحكم فيها اما على الموضوع الموجود في الخارج محققا نحو كل انسان حيوان بمعنى ان كل انسان موجود في الخارج حيوان كل في الخارج و اما على الموضوع الموجود في الخارج مقدرا نحو: كل انسان حيوان :بمعنى ان كل ما لو وجد في الخارج كان انسانا فهو على تقدير وجوده في الخارج حيوان و هذا الموجود المقدر انما اعتبروه في الافراد الممكنة لا الممتنعة كافراد اللاشيء و شريك البارى تعالى و اما على الموضوع الموجود في الذهن كقولنا: شريك الباري ممتنع بمعنى ان كلما يوجد في العقل ويفرضه العقل شريك البارى فهو موصوف في الذهن بالامتناع في الخارج و هذا انما اعتبروه في الموضوعات التي ليست لها افراد ممكنة التحقق في الخارج. قوله حرف السلب كـ لا و ليس وغيرهما مما يشاركهما في معنى السلب.
قوله من جزء اما من الموضوع فقط أو من المحمول فقط او من كليهما ثم القضية على الاول تسمى معدولة الموضوع و على الثاني تسمى معدولة المحمول و على الثالث تسمى معدولة الطرفين.
قوله معدولة: لان حرف السلب موضوع لسلب النسبة فاذا استعمل لا في هذا المعنى كان معدولا عن معناه الاصلى فسميت القضية التي هذا الحرف جزء من جزئها معدولة تسمية الكل باسم جزئه والقضية التي لا يكون حرف السلب جزء من طرفيها تسمى محصلة.
It is necessary in the affirmative [proposition] that the subject have existence, either actually — and it is external — or hypothetically — so it is real — or mentally — so it is mental. And the letter of negation may be made a part of one of the four parts, and nothing else, because the universal-negative and particular are equivalent, since whenever the judgment is true of the individuals of the subject in general, it is true of some of its individuals, and vice versa. So the universal-negative is included under the particular. And the singular is not investigated specifically, for there is no perfection in knowing particulars due to their change and lack of stability; rather, it is investigated within the quantified propositions that judge individuals collectively. And the natural [proposition] is not investigated in the sciences at all, for the universal natures in terms of their very concept — as their concept is the subject of natural science — not in terms of their realization within individuals, do not exist in the external world, so there is no perfection in knowing their states. So the considered propositions are restricted to the four categoricals.
His statement: 'It is necessary in the affirmative' i.e., in its truth. That is because the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of something for something, and the affirmation of something for something is a branch of the establishment of the thing for it — meaning the subject. So this judgment is true only if the subject is actually existent, either externally if the judgment is the affirmation of the predicate for it there, or in the mind likewise. Then the categorical propositions considered in the sciences, with respect to the existence of their subject, have three divisions: because the judgment is either regarding the subject existent externally actually, like 'Every human is an animal' meaning that every human existing externally is an animal — all in the external; or regarding the subject existent externally hypothetically, like 'Every human is an animal' meaning that everything that if it existed externally would be a human — so it is, upon the assumption of its existence externally, an animal — and this hypothetical existence is considered only for possible individuals, not impossible ones like the individuals of 'nothing' or 'partner of Allah Most High'; or regarding the subject existent in the mind, like our statement 'The partner of Allah is impossible' meaning that whatever exists in the mind and the mind supposes to be the partner of Allah is described in the mind as impossible in the external — and this is considered only for subjects that have no possible individuals to be realized externally. His statement: 'The letter of negation' like 'la' and 'laysa' and others that share with them the meaning of negation.
His statement: 'from part' — either from the subject only, or from the predicate only, or from both. Then the proposition according to the first is called 'deviated in subject', according to the second 'deviated in predicate', and according to the third 'deviated in both'. His statement: 'Deviated' because the letter of negation is placed for the negation of the relation; so if it is used in this meaning, it becomes deviated from its original meaning. So the proposition of which this letter is a part of its parts is called 'deviated' — naming the whole by its part. And the proposition of which the letter of negation is not a part of its two sides is called 'declared'.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for affirmative propositions and classifies them into four categoricals. The author first states that every affirmative proposition requires the subject to have existence, which can be actual (external), hypothetical (real), or mental. The letter of negation can be part of the four parts (subject, predicate, quantity, quality? Actually 'four parts' refers to the components of a categorical proposition). He then explains why the universal-negative (المهملة) is equivalent to the particular (الجزئية), so the universal-negative is subsumed under the particular. Singular propositions are not studied in logic because their knowledge is incomplete due to change. Natural propositions (about universal natures as such) are also excluded as they have no external existence. Thus only the four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are considered.
In the commentary, the author expands on the three types of subject existence: external (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' referring to actual humans), hypothetical (e.g., the same statement interpreted as applying if such humans existed, for possible individuals), and mental (e.g., 'The partner of Allah is impossible' where the subject exists only in the mind). He then explains the letter of negation (like 'la' and 'laysa') and how it can be part of the subject, predicate, or both, leading to deviated (معدولة) propositions: deviated in subject, deviated in predicate, or deviated in both. The term 'deviated' originates from the letter being used for negation of the relation rather than its original meaning. The opposite is 'declared' (محصلة) where the letter of negation is not part of the two sides (subject and predicate).

Deviated and Declared Propositions (معدولة و محصلة)
Yazdi explains the classification of categorical propositions into deviated and declared based on the placement of the negation letter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is deviated or declared based on the presence of a negation letter in its subject or predicate, and determine which part contains the negation.

Core Idea
When the letter of negation becomes part of the subject or predicate, the proposition is called deviated (معدولة); otherwise it is declared (محصلة).

In this part of the passage, Yazdi explains the classification of propositions based on whether the letter of negation (such as لا or ليس) is incorporated into the subject or predicate term. This distinction, often glossed over in introductory logic, is crucial for accurately analyzing the logical form of propositions. Understanding deviated vs. declared propositions helps avoid confusion between genuine negation of the predicate and a negative term. This topic is especially relevant in Islamic legal reasoning where the presence of negation in a term can affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the letter of negation
Recognize the Arabic letters like 'لا' and 'ليس' that indicate negation. Note that they can be made part of the subject or predicate.
2
Distinguish between deviated and declared propositions
If the negation letter is part of the subject or predicate term (i.e., attached to it), the proposition is deviated (معدولة); if it is not part of either term, it is declared (محصلة).
3
Classify the subtypes of deviated propositions
The negation can be in the subject only (معدولة الموضوع), predicate only (معدولة المحمول), or both (معدولة الطرفين).
4
Understand the reason for the name 'deviated'
The letter of negation is originally meant to negate the relation, so using it as part of a term deviates (عدل) it from its original meaning.

Distinguishing Matter and Modality in Modal Propositions
Yazdi defines modal propositions and distinguishes matter from modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the real modality (māddah) and the linguistic modality (jihah) in a modal proposition and determine whether they match for truth.

Core Idea
Truth in modal propositions requires that the linguistic modality match the real matter.

Modal propositions explicitly state the manner of the predicate's relation. But this stated modality must reflect reality. Yazdi distinguishes between the actual modality in reality (māddah) and the word or concept expressing it (jihah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the truth of modal statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality word in the proposition
Find words like 'necessarily', 'always', 'possibly' in the Arabic proposition. These indicate the linguistic modality (jihah).
The modality word is often a single term like 'bil-ḍarūra' (necessarily).
2
Determine the real matter
Consider the actual relationship between subject and predicate in reality. Is the predicate truly inseparable from the subject, or only sometimes? This reality is the māddah.
For example, 'Every human is an animal' is necessarily true in reality.
3
Compare modality to matter
If the linguistic modality (jihah) accurately describes the real modality (māddah), the proposition is true; otherwise false.
A false modal proposition would be 'Every human is necessarily a writer' because writing is not inseparable from humanness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القضية منها فتسمى معدولة والا فمحصلة و قد يصرح بكيفية النسبة فموجهة و ما به البيان جهة. فان كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مادام ذات الموضوع موجوداً فضرورية مطلقة او مادام وصفه فمشروطة عامة او في وقت معين فوقتية.
قوله بكيفية النسبة: اي نسبة المحمول الى الموضوع سواء كانت ايجابية أو سلبية تكون لا محالة مكيفة في نفس الامر و الواقع بكيفية مثل: الضرورة او الدوام او الامتناع او غير ذلك فتلك الكيفية الواقعة في نفس الأمر تسمى مادة القضية. ثم قد يصرح في القضيه بان تلك النسبة مكيفة في نفس الامر بكيفية كذا فالقضية حينئذ تسمى موجهة و قد لا يصرح بذلك فتسمى القضية مطلقة واللفظ الدال عليها في القضية الملفوظة والصورة العقلية الدالة عليها في القضية المعقولة يسمى جهة القضية فان طابقت الجهة المادة صدقت القضية كقولنا كل انسان حيوان بالضرورة والا كذبت كقولنا كل انسان بالضرورة.
قوله فان كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة: اي قد يكون الحكم في القضية الموجهة بان النسبة الثبوتية او السلبية ضرورية أي ممتنعة الانفكاك عن الموضوع على احد اربعة اوجه:
الأول: انها ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة نحو: كل انسان حيوان.
قوله: جزء من جزء منها : انما لم يقل: جزء منها : لأن حرف السلب جزء للقضية دائماً سواء كانت محصلة او معدولة.
On the divisions of the proposition: among them, if it is deviated, it is called deviated; otherwise, it is declared. And if the how of the relation is made explicit, then it is modal, and what expresses it is the modality. If the judgment in it has necessity of the relation as long as the essence of the subject exists, then it is absolute necessary; or as long as its description exists, then it is general conditional; or in a specific time, then it is temporal.
His statement: 'With respect to the how of the relation' i.e., the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether affirmative or negative, is necessarily characterized in reality by a modality such as necessity, permanence, impossibility, or other than that. So that modality occurring in reality is called the matter of the proposition. Then, sometimes it is made explicit in the proposition that that relation is characterized in reality by such a modality, so the proposition is then called modal; and if it is not made explicit, then the proposition is called absolute. And the word indicating it in the spoken proposition and the mental form indicating it in the mental proposition is called the modality of the proposition. If the modality matches the matter, the proposition is true, like our statement 'Every human is an animal necessarily'; otherwise it is false, like our statement 'Every human is necessarily [something else]'.
His statement: 'If the judgment in it has necessity of the relation' i.e., sometimes the judgment in the modal proposition is that the affirmative or negative relation is necessary, i.e., inseparable from the subject, in one of four ways: First: it is necessary as long as the essence of the subject exists, like 'Every human is an animal'.
His statement: 'part of its parts' — he did not say 'a part of it' because the letter of negation is always a part of the proposition, whether it is declared or deviated.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions as deviated (معدولة) or declared (محصلة), then introduces modal propositions. A proposition is modal (موجهة) when the manner (كيفية) of the predicate's relation to the subject is explicitly stated, such as necessity, permanence, or impossibility. The underlying reality of that modality is called the 'matter' (مادة) of the proposition. If no modality is expressed, the proposition is 'absolute' (مطلقة). The word or mental form expressing the modality is called the 'modality' (جهة) of the proposition; truth requires that the modality correspond to the actual matter. For example, 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is true because the relation is indeed necessary, while a false modality would make it false.
The author then explains the first type of necessity: a proposition is 'absolute necessary' (ضرورية مطلقة) when the predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject's essence exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'. Other types include 'general conditional' (مشروطة عامة) where the necessity holds only while a certain description remains, and 'temporal' (وقتية) for a specific time.
The final note clarifies that the letter of negation is always a part of the proposition, whether the proposition is declared or deviated; that is why the earlier phrase 'part of its parts' is used rather than 'a part of it'.

Key Terms
الموجهة
المادة
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When the proposition is expressed in Arabic

D) When the subject is a substance

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition's truth depends on the correspondence between the stated modality and the actual nature of the relation in reality.

Types of Necessity: Absolute, Conditional, and Temporal
Yazdi defines three types of necessity in modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify modal propositions into absolute necessary, general conditional, and temporal based on the condition of the subject's existence or description.

Core Idea
Necessity varies depending on whether it attaches to the subject's essence, description, or a specific time.

Yazdi explains three types of necessity in modal propositions: absolute necessary, general conditional, and temporal. Each type specifies a different condition under which the predicate holds necessarily. This classification is essential for accurately expressing and evaluating claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of necessity
Does the necessity hold as long as the subject's essence exists? Or only while a particular description remains? Or for a specific time period?
The condition is expressed in phrases like 'mā dāma al-dhāt' (as long as the essence), 'mā dāma al-wasf' (as long as the description), or 'fī waqt mu‘ayyan' (in a specific time).
2
Classify the proposition accordingly
If necessity is tied to the subject's essence, it is absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa). If tied to a description, it is general conditional (mashrūṭa ‘āmma). If tied to a specific time, it is temporal (waqtiyya).
Example: 'Every human is an animal' is absolute necessary because animality holds as long as the human essence exists.
3
Test with a counterexample
Consider a proposition like 'Every human is a writer necessarily'. Is the necessity absolute, conditional, or temporal? Clearly it is none, because writing is not necessary at all. This helps understand the conditions.
If the necessity is not genuine, the proposition is false.

Modified vs Unmodified Propositions
Author defines معدولة and محصلة propositions and explains the etymology of معدولة

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between modified (معدولة) and unmodified (محصلة) propositions and identify the type of modification (subject, predicate, or both).

Core Idea
A proposition is modified when the negative particle is inside the term, not when it negates the relation.

In this passage, the author draws a critical distinction between two types of propositions: those that contain a negative particle as part of their subject or predicate (معدولة) and those that do not (محصلة). Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate logical analysis, especially when dealing with modal propositions and definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the negative particle
Look for 'لا' (or similar) in the subject or predicate. If it appears as part of a term (e.g., اللاإنسان, لاكاتب), the proposition is معدولة. If the negation is in the copula (e.g., ليس, لا + verb), it is not.
2
Classify the modification type
Determine if the negative particle modifies the subject, predicate, or both. If only subject: معدولة الموضوع; only predicate: معدولة المحمول; both: معدولة الطرفين.
3
Contrast with محصلة
A proposition with no negative particle in either term is محصلة (unmodified). This includes affirmative and ordinary negative propositions.
Remember: 'Zayd is not writing' is محصلة because the negation is on the relation, not in the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القضية
منها (٥) فتسمى معدولة والا فمحصلة و قد يصرح بكيفية النسبة فموجهة و ما به البيان جهة. فان كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة مادام ذات الموضوع موجوداً فضرورية مطلقة او مادام وصفه فمشروطة عامة او في وقت معين فوقتية
والقضية على الاول تسمى معدولة الموضوع (۵۸) و على الثانى تسمى معدولة المحمول و على الثالث تسمى معدولة الطرفين.
قوله «معدولة»: لان حرف السلب موضوع لسلب النسبة فاذا استعمل لا في هذا المعنى كان معدولاً عن معناه الاصلى فسمّيت القضية التى هذا الحرف جزء من جزئها معدولة تسمية المكل باسم جزئه (الجزء خ (ل) والقضية التي لا يكون حرف السلب جزء من طرفيها (۵۹) تسمّى محصلة .
On the divisions of the proposition. Among them is that it is called 'modified' (معدولة), otherwise 'unmodified' (محصلة). And sometimes the modality of the relation is explicitly stated, so it is 'modal' (موجهة), and that by which it is expressed is the 'mode' (جهة). For if the judgment in it is about the necessity of the relation as long as the essence of the subject exists, then it is an 'absolute necessary' (ضرورية مطلقة); or as long as its description, then a 'general conditional' (مشروطة عامة); or in a specific time, then a 'temporal' (وقتية). And the proposition according to the first is called 'modified in subject'; according to the second, 'modified in predicate'; according to the third, 'modified in both parts'. His saying 'modified': because the negative particle is placed for negating the relation, so when 'لا' is used in this meaning it is diverted from its original meaning, so the proposition of which this particle is a part of its parts is called 'modified', naming the whole by its part. And the proposition that does not have the negative particle in either of its two parts is called 'unmodified'.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the types of propositions based on the inclusion of a negative particle in the subject or predicate. The author first enumerates the division: a proposition is either معدولة (modified) if it contains a negative particle in its subject or predicate (or both), or محصلة (unmodified) if it does not. Next, he introduces modal propositions (موجهة) where the modality (جهة) is explicitly stated, and gives four types depending on the duration or condition of the necessity: absolute necessary (ضرورية مطلقة), general conditional (مشروطة عامة), temporal (وقتية), and (later) absolute permanent (دائمة مطلقة) etc. However, the main focus of the commentary is on the term 'معدولة'. The super-commentator explains that the negative particle 'لا' is originally used to negate the relation between subject and predicate, but when it is used as a part of the subject or predicate itself (as in 'لا شيء' or not-man), it is diverted from its original function. Hence, the proposition that includes such a particle in its subject or predicate is called 'modified' after its part. The proposition lacking this particle in both extremes is 'unmodified'.

Key Terms
معدولة
محصلة

Key Takeaway
A proposition is modified (معدولة) when the negative particle is part of the subject or predicate term, distinguishing it from a regular negative proposition.

Material and Mode in Propositions
Author explains the modality and material of a proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the material (مادة) and mode (جهة) of a proposition and determine its truth based on their correspondence.

Core Idea
Truth depends on whether your words match reality's modality.

In this passage, the author clarifies two key aspects of every proposition: the real modality inherent in the subject-predicate relation (material) and its explicit linguistic expression (mode). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the truth of modal claims in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that every relation is qualified in reality
The relation between subject and predicate, whether affirmative or negative, always has a modality in reality—such as necessity, permanence, impossibility, etc. This is independent of any explicit statement.
This modality is the 'material' (مادة) of the proposition.
2
Identify the material (مادة) of a proposition
The material is the inherent quality of the relation as it exists in reality. For example, in 'Every human is an animal', the relation is necessary because humanity necessarily entails animality. That necessity is the material.
The material is not always stated explicitly.
3
Distinguish the mode (جهة) from the material
The mode is the explicit verbal or mental expression that indicates the modality, such as 'necessarily', 'permanently', 'impossibly'. It is the linguistic sign of the material.
A proposition that explicitly states the mode is called 'modal' (موجهة); one that does not is 'absolute' (مطلقة).
4
Check correspondence for truth
For a modal proposition to be true, the mode must match the actual material. If the mode correctly reflects reality, the proposition is true; otherwise, it is false. Example: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is true because the necessity is real; 'Every human is a writer necessarily' is false because writing is not necessary.
This is a key principle for evaluating modal statements in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « بكيفية النسبة»: اى نسبة المحمول الى الموضوع (۶۰) سواء كانت ايجابية أو سلبية تكون لا محالة مكيفة فى نفس الامر و الواقع بكيفية مثل: «الضرورة» او «الدوام» او «الامتناع» او غير ذلك فتلك الكيفية الواقعة فى نفس الأمر تسمى مادة القضية. (۶۱)
ثم قد يصرح فى القضيه بان تلك النسبة مكيفة فى نفس الامر بكيفية كذا فالقضية حينئذ تسمى «موجهة » (٦٢) و قد لا يصرح بذلك فتسمى القضية مطلقة )) (٦٣) والملفظ الدال عليها (٦٤) في القضية الملفوظة والصورة العقلية الدالة عليها في القضية المعقولة يسمى جهة القضية (٦٥) فان طابقت الجهة المادة (٦٦) صدقت القضية كقولنا كل انسان حیوان بالضرورة والا كذبت كقولنا كل انسان بالضرورة) .
His saying 'concerning the modality of the relation': i.e., the relation of the predicate to the subject, whether affirmative or negative, is necessarily qualified in reality and actuality by a quality such as 'necessity', 'permanence', 'impossibility', or other. That quality occurring in reality is called the 'material' (مادة) of the proposition. Then sometimes it is explicitly stated in the proposition that that relation is qualified in reality with such a quality; then the proposition is called 'modal' (موجهة). And sometimes it is not explicitly stated; then the proposition is called 'absolute' (مطلقة). And the verbal expression indicating it in the uttered proposition, and the mental form indicating it in the conceived proposition, is called the 'mode' (جهة) of the proposition. So if the mode corresponds to the material, the proposition is true, as when we say 'Every human is an animal necessarily'; otherwise it is false, as when we say 'Every human is necessarily'.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of 'material' (مادة) and 'mode' (جهة) in propositions. The super-commentator explains that the relation between predicate and subject is always qualified in reality by a modality such as necessity, permanence, impossibility, etc. This inherent quality is called the 'material' (مادة) of the proposition. The explicit verbal or mental expression of that modality is called the 'mode' (جهة). If the mode correctly reflects the material, the proposition is true; otherwise false. The example given is 'Every human is an animal necessarily'—here the necessity (الضرورة) is the material and the explicit word 'necessarily' is the mode, and it is true. The false example 'Every human is necessarily' is incomplete, but the point is that the mode (necessity) does not match the material (since not every human is necessarily a human? Actually it's a corrupted example; perhaps it means 'Every human is necessarily' without predicate, but the text is clear that the mode must correspond to the material for truth.

Key Takeaway

Four Types of ضرورية (Necessary) Propositions
Author enumerates and defines four kinds of necessary propositions based on duration and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of necessary propositions in modal logic based on their temporal or conditional restrictions.

Core Idea
Necessity in propositions can be absolute, conditional, temporal, or distributed.

In modal logic, not all necessary propositions are alike. The necessity of a relation between subject and predicate can be restricted by the subject's existence, a descriptive attribute, a specific time, or an indefinite time. This passage from the super-commentary explains these four types with crisp definitions and examples. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for analyzing the modality of statements in Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for each type
Read each definition and note what the necessity is tied to: the subject's existence (absolute), a descriptive attribute (conditional), a specific time (temporal), or an indefinite time (distributed).
2
Examine the examples
For each type, the author gives an example. Check how the example matches the condition. For instance, 'human is an animal' holds as long as the human exists.
3
Learn the Arabic names
Memorize the terms: ضرورية مطلقة, مشروطة عامة, وقتية مطلقة, منتشرة مطلقة. Notice how the name reflects the condition (e.g., 'mashrūṭa' for conditional).
4
Distinguish temporal from distributed
Compare the temporal (specific time) and distributed (some time) types. The temporal specifies a particular time (e.g., eclipse at opposition), while distributed is indefinite (e.g., human breathes at some time).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فان كان الحكم فيها بضرورة النسبة»: اى قد يكون الحكم في القضية الموجهة بان النسبة الثبوتية او السلبية (۶۷) ضرورية أي : ممتنعة الانفكاك عن الموضوع على احد اربعة اوجه : (۶۸)
الأول: انها ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة نحو: «كل انسان حيوان بالضرورة ولا شيء من الانسان بحجر بالضرورة فتسمى القضية حينئذ ضرورية مطلقة لاشتمالها على الضرورة (۶۹) و عدم تقييد الضرورة بالوصف او الوقت . (۷۰)
الثاني: انها ضرورية مادام الوصف العنوانى (۷۱) ثابت لذات الموضوع نحو: كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً ولا شيء منه بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً (۷۲) فتسمى ح مشروطة عامة لاشتراط الضرورة بالوصف العنوانى (۷۳) و لكون هذه القضية اعم من المشروطة الخاصة (۷۴) كما سيجيء.
الثالث: انها ضرورية في وقت معين نحو كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الارض بينه وبين الشمس (۷۵) ولا شي القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع (۷۶) فتسمى ح وقتية مطلقة (۷۷) لتقييد الضرورة بالوقت و عدم تقييد القضية باللادوام . (۷۸)
الرابع: انها ضرورية في وقت من الاوقات كقولنا: كل انسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتاًما و لا شيء منه بمتنفّس بالضرورة ،وقتاما، فتسمى ح منتشرة مطلقة لكون وقت الضرورة فيها منتشراً (۷۹) اى غير معين و عدم تقييد القضية باللادوام.
His saying 'for if the judgment in it is about the necessity of the relation': i.e., the judgment in the modal proposition may be that the affirmative or negative relation is necessary, i.e., impossible to separate from the subject, in one of four ways: first: it is necessary as long as the essence of the subject exists, like 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and 'Nothing of man is a stone necessarily'; then the proposition is called 'absolute necessary' (ضرورية مطلقة) because it includes necessity and no restriction of necessity by description or time. Second: it is necessary as long as the descriptive attribute (الوصف العنوانى) holds for the essence of the subject, like 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing' and 'Nothing of him is still in fingers necessarily as long as he is writing'; then it is called 'general conditional' (مشروطة عامة) because necessity is conditioned on the descriptive attribute and because this proposition is more general than the specific conditional as will come. Third: it is necessary in a specific time, like 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time when the earth interposes between it and the sun' and 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature'; then it is called 'absolute temporal' (وقتية مطلقة) because necessity is restricted to a time and the proposition is not restricted by non-permanence. Fourth: it is necessary at some time, like 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time' and 'Nothing of him breathes necessarily at some time'; then it is called 'absolute distributed' (منتشرة مطلقة) because the time of necessity is distributed, i.e., indefinite, and the proposition is not restricted by non-permanence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage explains four types of necessary propositions in modal logic. The super-commentator clarifies the statement from the original matn regarding the judgment of necessity. The necessity can be of four kinds, each with a specific condition. The first is 'absolute necessary' where the necessity holds as long as the subject's essence exists, e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' — this necessity is unconditional. The second is 'general conditional' where the necessity holds as long as a certain description remains attached to the subject, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily while he is writing'. The description is the attribute 'writer'. It is called 'general' because it includes all instances of the description, unlike the 'specific conditional' which will be explained later. The third is 'absolute temporal' where necessity is confined to a specific time, e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition'. The fourth is 'absolute distributed' where necessity holds at some indefinite time, e.g., 'Every human breathes necessarily at some time'. Each type gets its name from its condition: absolute, conditional, temporal, distributed.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa): A proposition where the necessity of the relation holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence without restriction. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
مشروطة عامة (mashrūṭa ʿāmma): A proposition where necessity holds as long as a certain descriptive attribute (عنوان) remains attached to the subject. [Note: The distinction between عامة and خاصة is explained later; here it is the general type.]
وقتية مطلقة (waqtiyya muṭlaqa): A proposition where necessity holds only for a specific, determined time. [Note: Term is consistent.]
منتشرة مطلقة (muntashira muṭlaqa): A proposition where necessity holds at some indefinite time (distributed time). [Note: This term is less common; sometimes called وقتية غير محصورة.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a مشروطة عامة proposition?
A) 'Kullu insānin ḥayawānun bi-ḍḍarūrati'
B) 'Kullu kātibin mutaḥarriku l-aṣābiʿi bi-ḍḍarūrati mā dām kātiban'
C) 'Kullu qamarin munkhasifun bi-ḍḍarūrati waqta l-istiqbāl'
D) 'Kullu insānin mutanaffisun bi-ḍḍarūrati waqtan mā'

Key Takeaway
The four types of necessary propositions—absolute, general conditional, absolute temporal, and absolute distributed—differ by what the necessity is tied to: the subject's existence, a descriptive attribute, a specific time, or an indefinite time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Logical Necessity from Permanence
Distinguishes ضرورة and دوام

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between logical necessity (ḍarūra) and permanence (dawām) by identifying whether separation is impossible or merely non‑occurrent.

Core Idea
Necessity is impossibility of separation; permanence is non‑separation that could have been otherwise.

In the logical analysis of modal propositions, two modes often appear similar: necessity and permanence. The super‑commentator Yazdi clarifies that the difference lies in the impossibility of separation. This distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of claims in kalām and uṣūl al‑fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of ضرورة and دوام in the passage. ضرورة is defined as 'استحالة انفكاك' (impossibility of separation), while دوام is 'عدم انفكاك لم يكن مستحيلاً' (non‑separation that is not impossible).
Pay attention to the key term 'استحالة' (impossibility) for ضرورة.
2
Compare the key difference
The critical distinction is that ضرورة involves impossibility, whereas دوام does not. In دوام, the separation is not impossible, meaning it could conceivably occur even if it never does.
This is like the motion of the celestial sphere: it moves permanently, but it is not impossible for it to stop (in some cosmologies).
3
Apply to the example
Consider the example given: 'دوام الحركة للفلك' (permanence of motion for the celestial sphere). The motion is constant, but it is not considered necessary because it is not impossible for the sphere to cease moving (in the Aristotelian framework, it is permanent but not necessary).
This example illustrates that دوام is a weaker modality than ضرورة.
4
Recognize the logical implication
Understand that دوام does not entail ضرورة. A proposition can be permanent without being necessary. This has implications for modal reasoning in Islamic sciences.
For instance, in kalām, arguments for the world's contingency often rely on necessity vs. permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «فدائمة مطلقة» والفرق بين الضرورة والدوام ان الضرورة هي استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء (۸۰) والدوام عدم انفکا كه لم يكن مستحيلاً كدوام الحركة للفلك. ثم الدوام اعنى عدم انفكاك النسبة الايجابية او السلبية عن الموضوع اما ذاتى او وصفى فان كان الحكم فى الموجّهة بالدوام الذاتى اى بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة سميت القضية «دائمة» لاشتمالها على الدوام و مطلقة» لعدم تقييد الدوام بالوصف العنوانى و ان كان الحكم بالدوام الوصفى اى بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن ذات الموضوع مادام الوصف العنواني ثابتاً تتلك الذات (۸۱) سميت «عرفية لان اهل العرف يفهمون هذا المعنى من القضية السالبة (۸۲) بل من الموجبة ايضاً (۸۳) عند الاطلاق (۸۴) فاذا قيل (۸۵): كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع فهموا ان هذا الحكم ثابت له ما دام كاتباً، و «عامة» لكونها اعم من العرفية الخاصة (۸۶) التي سيجيء ذكرها .
His saying 'absolute permanent' (فدائمة مطلقة). The difference between ضرورة (necessity) and دوام (permanence) is that necessity is the impossibility of separation of one thing from another, while permanence is non‑separation that is not impossible, like the permanence of motion for the celestial sphere. Then permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the affirmative or negative relation from the subject, is either essential (ذاتى) or descriptive (وصفى). So if the judgment in the modal proposition is about essential permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the relation from the subject as long as the essence of the subject exists, the proposition is called 'permanent' (دائمة) because it includes permanence, and 'absolute' (مطلقة) because permanence is not restricted by a descriptive attribute. And if the judgment is about descriptive permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the relation from the subject as long as the descriptive attribute holds for that essence, it is called 'customary' (عرفية) because people of custom ('urf) understand this meaning from the negative proposition, indeed from the affirmative as well when absolute; for if it is said: 'Every writer is moving his fingers', they understand that this judgment holds for him as long as he is writing. And 'general' (عامة) because it is more general than the specific customary (عرفية خاصة) which will be mentioned later.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between 'necessity' (ضرورة) and 'permanence' (دوام). The super-commentator clarifies that necessity implies the impossibility of separation, while permanence is merely de facto non‑separation without impossibility, e.g., the eternal motion of the heavens (according to some Aristotelian cosmology). Then he divides permanence into two types: essential permanence (دوام ذاتى) where the relation holds inseparably as long as the subject's essence exists, and descriptive permanence (دوام وصفى) where it holds as long as a certain description applies. A proposition with essential permanence is called 'absolute permanent' (دائمة مطلقة). A proposition with descriptive permanence is called 'customary general' (عرفية عامة) because it aligns with common understanding: when people say 'Every writer moves his fingers', they implicitly mean 'as long as he is writing'. The 'general' distinguishes it from the 'specific customary' (عرفية خاصة) which is more restricted (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers at the time of writing' with a specific time). The example illustrates that the relation is not necessary in the sense of impossibility of separation, but rather customary permanence.

Key Terms
ضرورة
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) ضرورة is stronger than دوام
B) ضرورة involves impossibility of separation while دوام does not
C) دوام is restricted to descriptive attributes
D) Both are identical in meaning

Key Takeaway
The core distinction is that ضرورة implies impossibility of separation, while دوام is mere absence of separation without impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
