Types of Permanent Propositions: مطلقة and عرفية
Defines دائمة مطلقة and عرفية عامة

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate between دائمة مطلقة and عرفية عامة propositions based on whether the permanence is linked to the subject's essence or a descriptive attribute.

Core Idea
دائمة مطلقة is permanent by essence; عرفية عامة is permanent only as long as a description holds.

After establishing the concept of permanence (دوام), the super‑commentator divides it into essential and descriptive. This yields two types of permanent modal propositions: absolute permanent and customary general. Understanding this division helps in interpreting modal claims in legal discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of دوام
In the passage, دوام is divided into duas types: دوام ذاتى (essential permanence) and دوام وصفى (descriptive permanence). Essential permanence means the relation holds as long as the subject's essence exists; descriptive permanence means it holds as long as a certain description applies.
The subject's essence is its universal nature, while description is an attribute that may not be essential.
2
See how they yield proposition types
Essential permanence yields the proposition type دائمة مطلقة (absolute permanent). Descriptive permanence yields عرفية عامة (customary general). The passage names them based on these conditions.
دائمة مطلقة is 'absolute' because permanence is not restricted by a descriptive attribute.
3
Analyze the example given
The example: 'كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع' (Every writer moves his fingers). People understand this as holding as long as he is a writer (descriptive permanence), not as long as his essence exists. This is why it is عرفية عامة.
A writer is not always a writer by essence; it is a descriptive attribute. So the relation is conditioned by that description.
4
Contrast with عرفية خاصة
The passage mentions عرفية خاصة (specific customary) which will come later. عرفية عامة is more general because it doesn't specify a time or condition beyond the description. This helps in understanding the 'general' in the name.
عرفية خاصة might add a temporal condition like 'at the time of writing'.

His saying 'absolute permanent' (فدائمة مطلقة). The difference between ضرورة (necessity) and دوام (permanence) is that necessity is the impossibility of separation of one thing from another, while permanence is non‑separation that is not impossible, like the permanence of motion for the celestial sphere. Then permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the affirmative or negative relation from the subject, is either essential (ذاتى) or descriptive (وصفى). So if the judgment in the modal proposition is about essential permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the relation from the subject as long as the essence of the subject exists, the proposition is called 'permanent' (دائمة) because it includes permanence, and 'absolute' (مطلقة) because permanence is not restricted by a descriptive attribute. And if the judgment is about descriptive permanence, i.e., non‑separation of the relation from the subject as long as the descriptive attribute holds for that essence, it is called 'customary' (عرفية) because people of custom ('urf) understand this meaning from the negative proposition, indeed from the affirmative as well when absolute; for if it is said: 'Every writer is moving his fingers', they understand that this judgment holds for him as long as he is writing. And 'general' (عامة) because it is more general than the specific customary (عرفية خاصة) which will be mentioned later.

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between 'necessity' (ضرورة) and 'permanence' (دوام). Four Types of Conditional Necessity and the Distinction Between Necessity and Perpetuity
Author defines four types of conditional necessity and contrasts necessity with perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the four types of conditional necessity in modal propositions and distinguish between necessity and perpetuity using the criteria defined in the text.

Core Idea
Necessity is impossibility of separation; perpetuity is mere non-separation.

Modal propositions in Islamic logic often qualify how a predicate relates to its subject. This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya defines four ways necessity can be conditioned—by attribute, by specific time, or by some time—and then distinguishes necessity (impossibility of separation) from perpetuity (actual non-separation). This distinction is crucial for understanding the strength of claims in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of conditional necessity
List the four types: 'ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa' (absolute necessary), 'mashrūṭa 'āmma' (conditioned general), 'waqtiyya muṭlaqa' (temporal absolute), and 'muntshira muṭlaqa' (scattered absolute). Note the Arabic names.
The first type (absolute necessary) is mentioned at the beginning: necessity unrestricted by description or time.
2
Extract the conditions for each type
For each type, note what restricts necessity: no restriction, attributive description (e.g., 'as long as he is a writer'), specific time (e.g., 'at opposition'), or unspecified time (e.g., 'at some time').
The example for 'mashrūṭa 'āmma' is 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.'
3
Distinguish necessity from perpetuity
Necessity (ḍarūra) is the impossibility of separation of predicate from subject. Perpetuity (dawām) is actual non-separation without the impossibility. The motion of the celestial sphere is perpetual but not necessary.
This distinction is explicitly made: 'al-ḍarūra hiya istiḥālat infikāk shay' 'an shay', wa al-dawām 'adam infikākihi lam yakun mustaḥīlan'.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider how this classification applies to theological arguments. For example, the claim that 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause' is necessary? Or is it perpetual?
In kalam, the necessity of the causal principle is debated. This classification helps articulate the modal force.

فتسمى القضية حينئذ ضرورية مطلقة لاشتمالها على الضرورة و عدم تقييد الضرورة بالوصف او الوقت. الثاني: انها ضرورية مادام الوصف العنوانى ثابت لذات الموضوع نحو: كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً ولا شيء منه بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً فتسمى حينئذ مشروطة عامة لاشتراط الضرورة بالوصف العنوانى و لكون هذه القضية اعم من المشروطة الخاصة كما سيجيء. الثالث: انها ضرورية في وقت معين نحو كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الارض بينه وبين الشمس ولا شيء القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع فتسمى حينئذ وقتية مطلقة لتقييد الضرورة بالوقت و عدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. الرابع: انها ضرورية في وقت من الاوقات كقولنا: كل انسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتاًما و لا شيء منه بمتنفّس بالضرورة وقتاًما فتسمى حينئذ منتشرة مطلقة لكون وقت الضرورة فيها منتشراً اى غير معين و عدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. قوله «فدائمة مطلقة» والفرق بين الضرورة والدوام ان الضرورة هي استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء والدوام عدم انفكاكه لم يكن مستحيلاً كدوام الحركة للفلك. ثم الدوام اعنى عدم انفكاك النسبة الايجابية او السلبية عن الموضوع اما ذاتى او وصفى فان كان الحكم فى الموجّهة بالدوام الذاتى اى بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة سميت القضية «دائمة» لاشتمالها على الدوام و «مطلقة» لعدم تقييد الدوام بالوصف العنوانى و ان كان الحكم بالدوام الوصفى اى بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن ذات الموضوع مادام الوصف العنواني ثابتاً لتلك الذات سميت «عرفية عامة» لان اهل العرف يفهمون هذا المعنى من القضية السالبة بل من الموجبة ايضاً عند الاطلاق فاذا قيل: كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع فهموا ان هذا الحكم ثابت له ما دام كاتباً، و «عامة» لكونها اعم من العرفية الخاصة التي سيجيء ذكرها.
Then the proposition is called 'necessary absolute' because it contains necessity and the necessity is not restricted by description or time. Second: it is necessary as long as the attributive description is established for the subject of the subject, like: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Nothing from him is still in fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer.' Then it is called 'conditioned general' because necessity is conditioned by the attributive description, and because this proposition is more general than the 'conditioned specific' as will come. Third: it is necessary in a specific time, like: 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun' and 'Nothing of the moon is necessarily eclipsed at the time of quadrature.' Then it is called 'temporal absolute' because necessity is restricted to time and the proposition is not restricted by non-perpetuity. Fourth: it is necessary at some time, like: 'Every human is necessarily breathing at some time' and 'Nothing from him is necessarily breathing at some time.' Then it is called 'scattered absolute' because the time of necessity in it is scattered, i.e., unspecified, and the proposition is not restricted by non-perpetuity. His saying 'perpetual absolute': The difference between necessity and perpetuity is that necessity is the impossibility of separation of something from something, while perpetuity is non-separation without it being impossible, like the perpetuity of motion for the celestial sphere. Then perpetuity, i.e., non-separation of the affirmative or negative relation from the subject, is either essential or attributive. If the judgment in the modal is by essential perpetuity, i.e., by non-separation of the relation from the subject as long as the subject's essence exists, the proposition is called 'perpetual' because it contains perpetuity, and 'absolute' because perpetuity is not restricted by the attributive description. And if the judgment is by attributive perpetuity, i.e., by non-separation of the relation from the subject's essence as long as the attributive description is established for that essence, it is called 'general customary' because people of custom understand this meaning from the negative proposition, even from the affirmative as well when unrestricted. So if it is said: 'Every writer is moving his fingers,' they understand that this judgment is established for him as long as he is a writer. And 'general' because it is more general than the 'specific customary' which will be mentioned.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies modal propositions based on how necessity ('darura') is qualified. The author presents four types. The first type, mentioned at the start, is 'necessary absolute' (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa) where necessity is not restricted by any description or time; it simply asserts a necessary connection as long as the subject exists. The second type is 'conditioned general' (mashrūṭa 'āmma): necessity holds as long as a specific attributive description (al-waṣf al-'unwānī) applies to the subject. The example given is 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'—the necessity of finger movement is tied to the attribute of being a writer. The third type is 'temporal absolute' (waqtiyya muṭlaqa): necessity holds only at a specific time, like the moon's eclipse at opposition. The fourth is 'scattered absolute' (muntshira muṭlaqa): necessity holds at some unspecified time, like breathing at some time. After enumerating these, the author distinguishes between 'darura' (necessity) and 'dawām' (perpetuity). Necessity implies the impossibility of separation; perpetuity is actual non-separation without implying impossibility. The motion of the celestial sphere is perpetual but not necessary. Then subcategories of perpetuity are given: essential perpetuity (dā'ima muṭlaqa) where the judgment holds as long as the subject's essence exists, and attributive perpetuity ('urfiyya 'āmma) where the judgment holds as long as the attribute exists, which is understood by common custom from unrestricted statements like 'Every writer moves his fingers.' The designation 'general' ('āmma) indicates that these propositions are broader than their more specific counterparts (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa, 'urfiyya khāṣṣa) that will be explained later.

Key Terms
ضرورة
دوام
مشروطة عامة

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'waqtiyya muṭlaqa' (temporal absolute) proposition?
A) Every human is necessarily breathing at some time.
B) Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.
C) Every moon is necessarily eclipsed at the time of opposition.
D) The celestial sphere is perpetually in motion.

Key Takeaway
Modal propositions are classified by how the judgment holds: unconditionally necessary, conditionally necessary, temporally specified, or perpetually without necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify the following propositions: (1) 'Every human is necessarily mortal' (2) 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer.' Identify which type of modal proposition each is and justify using the criteria from the lesson.
?

Simple Modal Propositions
Author defines three simple modal types and explains their names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of simple modal propositions (general absolute, general possible) and explain why they are called 'simple' and 'general'.

Core Idea
Simple modals each contain only one judgment—pure affirmation or pure negation.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational building blocks of modal propositions: the three simple types introduced by the author. Understanding these is essential because they form the basis for more complex modal statements used in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three simple types
Look at the passage: the author lists three simple modal propositions: 'general customary' (based on attributive perpetuity), 'general absolute' (based on actuality in one of three times), and 'general possible' (based on non-necessity of the opposite). Each is defined by a single criterion.
These three correspond to the basic modal judgments in Islamic logic.
2
Understand 'general absolute'
The 'general absolute' (muṭlaqa 'āmma) simply asserts that the relation actually holds in the past, present, or future, without any restriction of necessity or perpetuity. The author says it is 'what is understood from the proposition when unrestricted'.
Example: 'Zayd is sitting' — if unrestricted, it is general absolute.
3
Understand 'general possible'
The 'general possible' (mumkina 'āmma) asserts that the opposite of the relation is not necessary. For example, 'Zayd is a writer with possibility' means his not writing is not necessary.
This is possibility in the sense of non-necessity, not probability.
4
Confirm why they are simple
Each of these propositions contains only a single judgment: either pure affirmation (e.g., the relation exists) or pure negation (e.g., the opposite is not necessary). They are not mixed with a second judgment.
The author explicitly states that the eight modals (including these) are simple.

On the divisions of the proposition: the description, so it is 'general customary', or by its actuality, so it is 'general absolute', or by the non-necessity of its opposite, so it is 'general possible'. These are simple. And it may be restricted. His saying 'or by its actuality' means the relation is actualized in fact, so the 'general absolute' is that in which it is judged that the relation is actualized in fact, i.e., in one of the three times. It is called 'absolute' because this is what is understood from the proposition when it is unrestricted and not restricted by necessity or perpetuity or other aspects, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-perpetual and non-necessary existential, as will come. His saying 'or by the non-necessity of its opposite': when it is judged in the proposition that the opposite of the mentioned relation is not necessary, like our saying 'Zayd is a writer with possibility', meaning that writing is not impossible for him, i.e., its negation is not necessary. The proposition then is 'possible' because it contains possibility, which is the negation of necessity, and 'general' because it is more general than the 'specific possible'. His saying 'these are simple' means the eight mentioned propositions from the set of modals are simple. Know that the modal proposition is either simple, and it is that whose reality is either only affirmation or only negation, as passed in the eight modals, or composite, and it is that whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, on condition that the second part in it is not mentioned in an independent expression, whether there is composition in the wording, like our saying 'Every human is laughing in actuality, not perpetually', so our saying 'not perpetually' is an indication of a negative judgment, i.e., 'Nothing from the human is laughing in actuality', or there is no composition in the wording, like our saying 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility', and it is in meaning two general possible propositions: i.e., 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'Nothing from the human is a writer with general possibility', and consideration is given to affirmation and negation according to the first part which is the origin of the proposition. And know also that the composite proposition is only obtained by restricting a simple proposition with a restriction like non-perpetuity and non-necessity.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between simple (basīṭa) and composite (murakkaba) modal propositions. It begins by introducing three types of simple modals: 'general customary' (based on attributive perpetuity), 'general absolute' (based on actuality in one of three times), and 'general possible' (based on the non-necessity of the opposite). These are called 'simple' because each contains only one judgment (affirmation or negation). The author then explains each: the 'general absolute' (muṭlaqa 'āmma) is the understanding of a proposition when unrestricted by modality—it simply asserts actuality in the past, present, or future. The 'general possible' (mumkina 'āmma) asserts that the opposite of the relation is not necessary, i.e., possibility in the sense of non-necessity. The eight modals referred to are the basic types: necessary, perpetual, conditional, temporal, scattered, etc., all of which are simple. Then the author introduces composite propositions (murakkaba), which combine affirmative and negative judgments into a single proposition without independent expression of the second part. For example, 'Every human is laughing in actuality, not perpetually' is composite: it affirms laughing in actuality and implicitly negates perpetuity. The phrase 'not perpetually' is not a separate proposition but a qualifier. Another example: 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' is composite in meaning, as it entails both a general possible affirmation and a general possible negation. The composite proposition is formed by restricting a simple proposition with a qualifier like 'non-perpetuity' (lā dawām) or 'non-necessity' (lā ḍarūra).

Key Terms
بسيطة (basīṭa)
مطلقة عامة (muṭlaqa 'āmma)
ممكنة عامة (mumkina 'āmma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is not restricted by time.
D) Because it is the opposite of necessity.

Key Takeaway
Simple modal propositions contain a single judgment (affirmation or negation) and include the general absolute and general possible as key types for unrestricted and non-necessary claims.

Propositions

Composite Modal Propositions
Author explains how composite propositions are formed by restricting simple propositions with modifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify composite modal propositions and construct them by restricting a simple proposition with a qualifier like 'non-perpetuity' or 'non-necessity'.

Core Idea
Composite modals combine affirmation and negation into one proposition without independent clauses.

Having learned simple modals, we now turn to composite modal propositions, which are formed by adding a qualifying phrase that implicitly adds a second judgment. This lesson explains how to recognize and form such propositions, which are common in precise theological and legal statements.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish composite from simple
The passage states that a composite proposition's reality is composed of affirmation and negation, but the second part is not expressed independently. Simple modals have only one judgment.
Example: 'Every human is laughing in actuality, not perpetually' includes both an affirmation and a negation.
2
Identify the condition for composition
The author stipulates that the second part (the negation) must not be a separate independent clause. It can be a phrase like 'not perpetually' or implied by a term like 'specific possibility'.
This distinguishes composite from two separate propositions.
3
Analyze the example with 'not perpetually'
In 'Every human is laughing in actuality, not perpetually', the phrase 'not perpetually' indicates a negative judgment: that the human is not laughing perpetually. This negative judgment is not a separate statement but a qualifier.
The proposition is one composite, not two simple ones.
4
Analyze the example with 'specific possibility'
The passage says 'Every human is a writer with specific possibility' is composite in meaning because it entails two general possible judgments: that every human is a writer with general possibility, and that no human is a writer with general possibility. The second part is not stated explicitly.
This shows that composition can be implicit in modal terms.

Propositions

5 min
The Three Simple Modal Propositions
Author defines three simple modal propositions and explains their meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between general customary, general absolute, and general possible propositions and identify each in a given example.

Core Idea
Three simple modals hinge on actuality, unrestricted actuality, or non-necessity of the contrary.

The passage defines three foundational modal propositions in Islamic logic: al-urfiyya al-amma, al-mutlaqa al-amma, and al-mumkina al-amma. Understanding these is essential for evaluating arguments with modal force, such as those in kalam about necessity and contingency. The commentator expands on each, clarifying their logical conditions and the rationale for their names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Types
From the passage, locate the three modal propositions: 'urfiyya 'amma, mutlaqa 'amma, and mumkina 'amma. Note their Arabic names and the order in which they are introduced.
2
Extract the Defining Condition for Each
For each type, identify the key logical condition: (1) general customary is based on actual description (bi-fi'liyyatiha), (2) general absolute judges the relation as actually realized in one of the three times, (3) general possible judges that the contrary is not necessary.
3
Understand the Naming
Why 'mutlaqa' (absolute)? The passage says it is the default meaning of a proposition when unrestricted. Why 'amma' (general)? Each is more general than a more specific counterpart: general absolute is broader than non-permanent and non-necessary existential; general possible is broader than particular possible.
4
Recognize Their Simplicity
The passage states 'these are simple' (basā'iṭ), meaning they are not compounded from affirmation and negation. This distinguishes them from compound modals.

On the divisions of the proposition: The descriptive is general customary, or by its actuality is general absolute, or by the non-necessity of its contrary is general possible. These are simple. And it may be restricted. His statement: 'or by its actuality' means the relation is actually realized, so the general absolute is that in which it is judged that the relation is actually realized, i.e., in one of the three times (87), and it is called 'absolute' because this is the meaning of the proposition when unrestricted (88) and not qualified by necessity, permanence, or other aspects, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-permanent and non-necessary existential (89) as will come. His statement: 'or by the non-necessity of its contrary' – if it is judged in the proposition that the contrary of the mentioned relation (90) is not necessary, like our saying 'Zayd is a writer possibly', meaning writing is not impossible for him (91), meaning its negation is not necessary for him, then the proposition is possible (92) because it includes possibility, which is the negation of necessity (93), and 'general' because it is more general than the particular possible (94). His statement: 'these are simple' – i.e., the eight mentioned propositions (95) among the modals (96) are simple.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on three types of simple modal propositions. The matn states that the descriptive proposition (al-wasf) is either general customary (urfiyya 'amma) when the description is based on actuality, or general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma) when the judgment is based on actuality in one of the three times, or general possible (mumkina 'amma) when the contrary is not necessary. The commentator explains each: 'general absolute' means the relation is actually realized in one of the three times (past, present, future) and is called 'absolute' because it lacks further qualification; 'general' because it is broader than the non-permanent and non-necessary existential. The 'general possible' is explained as a judgment that the opposite of the relation is not necessary; the example 'Zayd is a writer possibly' means writing is not impossible, i.e., its negation is not necessary. It is called 'possible' because it entails possibility (imkan), which is the negation of necessity, and 'general' because it is more general than the particular possible. Finally, the commentator notes that these three (along with five others) are considered simple modals, meaning they are not compounded from affirmation and negation. This establishes the foundational modals used in further discussions.

Compound Modal Propositions: Definition and Structure
Author defines compound modal propositions and their formation by restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish compound modal propositions from simple ones and identify the condition that the second part is not independently stated.

Core Idea
A compound modal proposition combines affirmation and negation with an implied second part.

In this passage, the author distinguishes simple from compound modal propositions. A compound modal has a truth value composed of both affirmation and negation, but the second part is not expressed as an independent statement. This lesson will help you recognise such propositions and understand how they arise from restricting simple modals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of compound modal
Find the author's statement: 'واعلم ان القضية الموجهة اما بسيطة ... واما مركبة'. Note that compound means its truth is compounded from affirmation and negation.
The phrase 'مركبة من الايجاب والسلب' is key.
2
Check the condition
The second part must not be mentioned in an independent phrase. Compare the two examples: 'كل انسان ضاحك بالفعل لا دائماً' has an explicit negative phrase, while 'كل انسان كاتب بالامكان الخاص' has no separate phrase but implies a negation.
The condition ensures the proposition is considered one compound, not two separate statements.
3
Analyze the examples
Work through each example: the first is compound in wording, the second is compound in meaning. Understand that the primary judgment (affirmation or negation) is taken from the first part.
The author says 'العبرة بالايجاب والسلب بالجزء الاول'.
4
Recognize the restriction mechanism
Compound propositions arise by restricting simple propositions with 'لا دوام' (non-permanence) or 'لا ضرورة' (non-necessity). This sets up the four subtypes.
This will be expanded in the next lesson.

Know that the modal proposition is either simple (97), which is whose truth is either only affirmation or only negation as passed in the eight modals, or compound, which is whose truth (98) is compounded from affirmation and negation (99) on condition that the second part is not mentioned in an independent phrase (100), whether there is in the wording (101) a composition like our saying 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' – so our saying 'not always' indicates a negative judgment, i.e., 'No human is laughing actually', or there is no composition in the wording like our saying 'Every human is a writer with particular possibility' which is in meaning two general possible propositions: i.e., 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. And consideration of affirmation and negation is by the first part which is the origin of the proposition (102). Know also that the compound proposition (103) is obtained only by restricting a simple proposition with a restriction (104) like non-permanence and non-necessity. The two general [customary] and the two temporal absolutes restricted by essential non-permanence are called particular conditional, particular customary, temporal, and widespread. And the general absolute may be restricted by essential non-necessity. His statement: 'the two general' – i.e., the general conditional and the general customary. His statement: 'and the two temporal' – i.e., the temporal absolute and the widespread absolute. His statement: 'by essential non-permanence' – and the meaning of essential non-permanence is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not permanent as long as the essence of the subject exists, so its opposite occurs certainly at some time (105), thus it indicates a proposition (106) which is a general absolute contrary to the original in quality but agreeing in quantity, so understand. (107) His statement: 'the particular conditional' – it is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence (108) like: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always', i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers actually' [large from prisoners? unclear]. His statement: 'and the particular customary' – it is the general customary restricted by essential non-permanence, like our saying: 'By permanence, no writer is motionless of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always', i.e., 'Every writer is motionless of fingers actually'. (109) His statement: 'and the temporal and widespread' – when the temporal absolute and the widespread absolute were restricted by essential non-permanence, the word 'absolute' was dropped from their names, so the first is called 'temporal' and the second 'widespread'. So the temporal is the temporal absolute restricted by essential non-permanence (110) like: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not always', i.e., 'No moon is eclipsed actually', and the widespread is the widespread absolute restricted by essential non-permanence like: 'No human is breathing necessarily at some time, not always', i.e., 'Every human is breathing actually'. His statement: 'by essential non-necessity' – and the meaning of essential non-necessity is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not necessary as long as the essence of the subject exists, so this (111) is a judgment on the possibility of its opposite, because possibility is the negation of necessity of the opposite side (112) as passed, so the import of essential non-necessity is a general possible (113) contrary to the original in quality. (114)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on compound modal propositions. It begins by distinguishing between simple and compound modals: a simple modal is purely affirmative or purely negative (as seen in the eight modals), while a compound modal has a truth that involves both affirmation and negation, provided the second part is not stated independently. The author gives examples: 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' is compound in wording because 'not always' is an independent expression indicating a negative judgment ('No human is laughing actually'). Alternatively, a proposition like 'Every human is a writer with particular possibility' is compound in meaning even though the wording is simple, because it implies two general possible propositions: an affirmative ('Every human is a writer with general possibility') and a negative ('No human is a writer with general possibility'). The primary judgment (affirmation or negation) is taken from the first part. The author then states that compound propositions arise from restricting simple propositions with 'non-permanence' (lā dawām) or 'non-necessity' (lā ḍarūra). The commentary elaborates on four specific subtypes: (1) al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa (particular conditional) is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not always' – this means the opposite occurs at some time. (2) al-urfiyya al-khāṣṣa (particular customary) is the general customary restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'No writer is motionless of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' – meaning every writer is actually motionless of fingers. (3) al-waqtiyya (temporal) is the temporal absolute restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not always' – meaning no moon is actually eclipsed. (4) al-muntashira (widespread) is the widespread absolute restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'No human is breathing necessarily at some time, not always' – meaning every human is actually breathing. Finally, the passage explains essential non-necessity: it means the relation is not necessary as long as the subject exists, so the opposite is possible (general possible). This is a judgment contrary in quality to the original.

لادوام ذاتي

The Four Subtypes of Compound Modals
Author explains the four subtypes of compound modal propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four compound modal subtypes (particular conditional, particular customary, temporal, widespread) and provide their definitions and examples.

Core Idea
Four compound modals arise by restricting general conditionals, customaries, and absolutes with essential non-permanence.

In this passage, the author specifies four subtypes of compound modal propositions: al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa (particular conditional), al-urfiyya al-khāṣṣa (particular customary), al-waqtiyya (temporal), and al-muntashira (widespread). Each is formed by restricting a general or absolute simple modal with the phrase 'essential non-permanence' (lā dawām dhātī). This lesson will help you distinguish these four types and apply them in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base propositions
Remember the general conditional (المشروطة العامة), general customary (العرفية العامة), temporal absolute (الوقتية المطلقة), and widespread absolute (المنتشرة المطلقة). These are the simple modals that will be restricted.
These were covered in previous lessons on simple modals.
2
Understand the restriction: essential non-permanence
Essential non-permanence means the relation in the proposition is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists; the opposite occurs at some time. This is a key modifier.
The text says: 'ومعنى اللادوام الذاتي ان هذه النسبة ... ليست دائمة مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة'.
3
Apply the restriction to each base
For each base proposition, add 'lā dawām dhātī' and observe the name change: the two general propositions become 'particular' (خاصة), and the two absolutes drop 'absolute' and become 'temporal' and 'widespread'.
The naming convention is important: the absolute modals lose the word 'مطلقة'.
4
Match examples to subtypes
Work through each example given in the passage: for particular conditional, 'كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً لا دائماً'. For particular customary, 'لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً'. For temporal, 'كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت الحيلولة لا دائماً'. For widespread, 'لا شيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالضرورة وقتاً ما لا دائماً'. Understand how each expresses both an affirmative and negative aspect.
Note that the first part gives the original judgment, and the second part ('لا دائماً') negates it.

كقولنا: بالدوام لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، اى كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل . (۱۰۹)
قوله « والوقتية والمنتشرة»»: لما قيدت الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة باللادوام الذاتي، حذف من اسميهما لفظ الاطلاق فسميت الأولى وقتية والثانية منتشرة، فالوقتية هي الوقتية المطلقة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتى (۱۱۰) نحو: كل قمر منخف بالضرورة وقت الحيلولة لادائماً، أى لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالفعل والمنتشرة هي المنتشرة المطلقة المقيدة باللادواه الذاتي نحو: لاشيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالضرورة وقتأم لا دائماً :اى كل انسان متنفس به لفعل.
قوله « باللاضرورة الذاتية»: ومعنى اللاضرورة الذاتية ان هذه النسبة المذكورة في القضية ليست ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة فيكون هذا (۱۱۱) حكماً بامكان نقيضها لان الامكان هـ سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل (۱۱۲) كمامر فيكون مفاد اللاضرورة الذاتية ممكنة عامة (۱۱۳) مخالفة للاصل في الكيف . (۱۱۴)
Know that the modal proposition is either simple (97), which is whose truth is either only affirmation or only negation as passed in the eight modals, or compound, which is whose truth (98) is compounded from affirmation and negation (99) on condition that the second part is not mentioned in an independent phrase (100), whether there is in the wording (101) a composition like our saying 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' – so our saying 'not always' indicates a negative judgment, i.e., 'No human is laughing actually', or there is no composition in the wording like our saying 'Every human is a writer with particular possibility' which is in meaning two general possible propositions: i.e., 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. And consideration of affirmation and negation is by the first part which is the origin of the proposition (102). Know also that the compound proposition (103) is obtained only by restricting a simple proposition with a restriction (104) like non-permanence and non-necessity. The two general [customary] and the two temporal absolutes restricted by essential non-permanence are called particular conditional, particular customary, temporal, and widespread. And the general absolute may be restricted by essential non-necessity. His statement: 'the two general' – i.e., the general conditional and the general customary. His statement: 'and the two temporal' – i.e., the temporal absolute and the widespread absolute. His statement: 'by essential non-permanence' – and the meaning of essential non-permanence is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not permanent as long as the essence of the subject exists, so its opposite occurs certainly at some time (105), thus it indicates a proposition (106) which is a general absolute contrary to the original in quality but agreeing in quantity, so understand. (107) His statement: 'the particular conditional' – it is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence (108) like: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always', i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers actually' [large from prisoners? unclear]. His statement: 'and the particular customary' – it is the general customary restricted by essential non-permanence, like our saying: 'By permanence, no writer is motionless of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always', i.e., 'Every writer is motionless of fingers actually'. (109) His statement: 'and the temporal and widespread' – when the temporal absolute and the widespread absolute were restricted by essential non-permanence, the word 'absolute' was dropped from their names, so the first is called 'temporal' and the second 'widespread'. So the temporal is the temporal absolute restricted by essential non-permanence (110) like: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not always', i.e., 'No moon is eclipsed actually', and the widespread is the widespread absolute restricted by essential non-permanence like: 'No human is breathing necessarily at some time, not always', i.e., 'Every human is breathing actually'. His statement: 'by essential non-necessity' – and the meaning of essential non-necessity is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not necessary as long as the essence of the subject exists, so this (111) is a judgment on the possibility of its opposite, because possibility is the negation of necessity of the opposite side (112) as passed, so the import of essential non-necessity is a general possible (113) contrary to the original in quality. (114)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on compound modal propositions. It begins by distinguishing between simple and compound modals: a simple modal is purely affirmative or purely negative (as seen in the eight modals), while a compound modal has a truth that involves both affirmation and negation, provided the second part is not stated independently. The author gives examples: 'Every human is laughing actually, not always' is compound in wording because 'not always' is an independent expression indicating a negative judgment ('No human is laughing actually'). Alternatively, a proposition like 'Every human is a writer with particular possibility' is compound in meaning even though the wording is simple, because it implies two general possible propositions: an affirmative ('Every human is a writer with general possibility') and a negative ('No human is a writer with general possibility'). The primary judgment (affirmation or negation) is taken from the first part. The author then states that compound propositions arise from restricting simple propositions with 'non-permanence' (lā dawām) or 'non-necessity' (lā ḍarūra). The commentary elaborates on four specific subtypes: (1) al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa (particular conditional) is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not always' – this means the opposite occurs at some time. (2) al-urfiyya al-khāṣṣa (particular customary) is the general customary restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'No writer is motionless of fingers as long as he is a writer, not always' – meaning every writer is actually motionless of fingers. (3) al-waqtiyya (temporal) is the temporal absolute restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not always' – meaning no moon is actually eclipsed. (4) al-muntashira (widespread) is the widespread absolute restricted by essential non-permanence; example: 'No human is breathing necessarily at some time, not always' – meaning every human is actually breathing. Finally, the passage explains essential non-necessity: it means the relation is not necessary as long as the subject exists, so the opposite is possible (general possible). This is a judgment contrary in quality to the original.

Understanding the Four Restricted Propositions
Author defines four restricted propositions via la-dawam al-dhati

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of restricted propositions based on the condition of essential non-permanence.

Core Idea
Essential non-permanence transforms general propositions into restricted ones by negating permanence.

This lesson unpacks how the concept of essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati) is applied to four types of general propositions to yield the 'special' versions: al-mashruta al-khassa, al-'urfiyya al-khassa, al-waqtiyya, and al-muntashira. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analysing compound propositions in Islamic logic, particularly in the study of conditional and temporal statements used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Grasp essential non-permanence
Understand that 'la-dawam dhati' means the predicate relation is not permanent for the entire existence of the subject; its opposite occurs at some time. It does not mean the proposition is false, but that the predicate does not hold at all times.
This concept is key to all four definitions.
2
Identify the four base propositions
Recall the four general propositions: general conditional (mashruta 'amma), general customary ('urfiyya 'amma), absolute temporal (waqtiyya mutlaqa), absolute widespread (muntashira mutlaqa). Each has a specific mode and quantifier.
Refer to earlier lessons if needed.
3
Apply la-dawam to each base
See how adding la-dawam dhati changes each: mashruta 'amma becomes mashruta khassa; 'urfiyya 'amma becomes 'urfiyya khassa; waqtiyya mutlaqa becomes waqtiyya; muntashira mutlaqa becomes muntashira. The restriction ensures the predicate holds only under the condition, not permanently.
The names lose 'mutlaqa' for the last two.
4
Examine the examples
For mashruta khassa: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not permanently.' For 'urfiyya khassa: 'No writer has his fingers still as long as he is writing, not permanently.' For waqtiyya: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not permanently.' For muntashira: 'No human is breathing necessarily at any time, not permanently.' Note the quality and quantifier changes.
The examples clarify the logical force of each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « العامتان»: اى المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة . قوله « والوقتيتان» أى الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة . قوله « باللادوام الذاتى» ومعنى اللادواء الذاتي ان هذه النسبة المذكورة فى القضية ليست دائمة مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة فيكون نقيضها واقعاً البتة في زمان من الازمنة فيكون اشارة الى قضية مطلقة عامة مخالفة للاصل في الكيف موافقة له في الكم فافهم. قوله «المشروطة الخاصة»: هي المشروطة العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتي نحو: كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، أى: لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل كبير من اسرى قوله «والعرفية الخاصة»: هي العرفية العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتي كقولنا: بالدوام لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً، اى كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل . قوله « والوقتية والمنتشرة»»: لما قيدت الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة باللادوام الذاتي، حذف من اسميهما لفظ الاطلاق فسميت الأولى وقتية والثانية منتشرة، فالوقتية هي الوقتية المطلقة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتى نحو: كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت الحيلولة لادائماً، أى لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف بالفعل والمنتشرة هي المنتشرة المطلقة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتي نحو: لاشيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالضرورة وقتأم لا دائماً :اى كل انسان متنفس به لفعل.
His saying 'the two general ones': i.e., the general conditional and the general customary. His saying 'and the two temporal ones': i.e., the absolute temporal and the absolute widespread. His saying 'by the essential non-permanence': and the meaning of essential non-permanence is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not permanent as long as the essence of the subject exists, so its contradictory necessarily occurs at some time, thus it indicates an absolute general proposition differing from the original in quality and agreeing with it in quantity, so understand. His saying 'the special conditional': it is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence, like: Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not permanently, i.e., no writer is actually moving his fingers except? Commentary
This passage defines four types of propositions that result from restricting the four general types (al-mashruta al-'amma, al-'urfiyya al-'amma, al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa, al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) with the condition of essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati). The author first explains that 'essential non-permanence' means the relation is not permanent for the entire duration of the subject's existence, so its negation occurs at some time. This indicates an absolute general proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) that differs from the original in quality (positive vs. negative) but agrees in quantity. Then he defines each: al-mashruta al-khassa is the general conditional restricted by essential non-permanence, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing, not permanently'—meaning no writer is actually moving his fingers all the time. Note the example is a bit garbled in OCR. Al-'urfiyya al-khassa is the general customary restricted similarly, e.g., 'No writer has fingers still as long as he is writing, not permanently'—meaning every writer actually moves his fingers? Actually the example states a negation: 'No writer has still fingers' but the translation may be inconsistent. Next, al-waqtiyya and al-muntashira are the absolute temporal and absolute widespread minus the word 'absolute' when restricted. Al-waqtiyya: e.g., 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition, not permanently'—meaning no moon is actually eclipsed at all times. Al-muntashira: e.g., 'No human is breathing necessarily at any time, not permanently'—meaning every human actually breathes. These definitions clarify the technical terminology used in propositional logic, especially for compound propositions.

Definition of al-Wujudiyya al-Ḍarūriyya
Author defines al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya and decompose it into an absolute general and a general possible proposition.

Core Idea
Al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya is a compound: an absolute general plus a general possible.

The author defines a new proposition type by restricting the absolute general with essential non-necessity. Understanding this compound requires recognizing that 'not necessarily' implies the possibility of the contradictory, yielding two components: one affirmative and one negative.

Guided Path
1
Understand essential non-necessity
Essential non-necessity (al-la-darura al-dhatiyya) means the predicate relation is not necessary for the entire existence of the subject, so its contradictory is possible.
Possibility here is general possibility (imkan 'amm).
2
Recognize the two components
The absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) affirms the predicate at some time. The restriction 'not necessarily' adds a general possible proposition negating necessity.
3
Combine them into one compound
Al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya is the conjunction of an affirmative absolute general and a negative general possible: the predicate holds at some time but it is not necessary that it holds.
Example: 'Every human is actually breathing, not necessarily' means 'Some human is breathing at some time' (absolute general) and 'It is possible that a human does not breathe' (general possible).
4
Verify the composition
Check that the two parts are consistent: the absolute general is affirmative, the general possible is negative (since 'not necessarily' = possibility of not). This differs from the original absolute general which has no modal component.
The compound is considered a valid (mu'tabar) proposition type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « باللاضرورة الذاتية»: ومعنى اللاضرورة الذاتية ان هذه النسبة المذكورة في القضية ليست ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة فيكون هذا حكماً بامكان نقيضها لان الامكان هو سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل كما مر فيكون مفاد اللاضرورة الذاتية ممكنة عامة مخالفة للاصل في الكيف . قوله فتسمى الوجودية اللاضرورية»: لان معنى المطلقة العامة هي فعلية النسبة و وجودها فى وقت من الاوقات ولاشتمالها على اللاضرورة، فالوجودية اللاضرورية هى المطلقة العامة المقيدة بالملاضرورة الذاتية نحو كل انسان متنفس بالفعل لا بالضرورة اى لاشيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالامكان العام فهى مركبة من مطلقة عامة و ممكنة عامة احديهما موجبة والاخرى سالبة . قوله «او باللادوام الذاتى»: انما قيد اللادوام بالذاتي، لان تقييد العامتين باللادوام الوصفى غير صحيح ضرورة تنا فى اللادوام بحسب الوصف مع الدوام بحسب الوصف نعم يمكن تقييد الوقتيتين المطلقتين باللادوام الوصفى لكن هذا التركيب غير معتبر عندهم. و اعلم انه كما يصح تقييد هذه القضاياء الاربع باللادوام الذاتي ، كذلك يصح تقييدها باللاضرورية الذاتية و كذلك تقييد ما سوى المشروطة العامة يصح تلك من الجملة باللاضرورة الوصفية فالاحتمالات الحاصلة من ملاحظة كل من تلك القضاياء الاربع مع كل من تلك القيود الاربعة ستة عشر ثلاثة منها غير صحيحة و اربعة منها صحيحة معتبرة و التسعة الباقية صحيحة غير معتبرة. و اعلم ايضاً انه كما يمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللادوام و اللاضرورة الذاتيتين، كذلك يمكن تقييدها باللادوام و اللاضرورة الوصفيتين و هذان ايضاً من الاحتمالات الصحيحة الغير المعتبرة و كما يصح تقييد الممكنة العامة باللاضرورة الذاتية، كذلك يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية وكذا باللادوام الذاتي والوصفى لكن هذه الاحتمالات الثلاثة ايضاً غير معتبرة عندهم. و ينبغى ان يعلم ان التركيب لا ينحصر فما اشرنا اليه بل سيجيء الاشارة الى بعض آخر و يمكن تركيبات كثيرة اخرى لم يتعرضوا لها لكن المتنبه بعد التنبيه بما ذكروه يتمكن من استخراج ای قدر شاء. قوله فتسمى الوجودية اللادائمة»: هى المطلقة العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتى نحو لا شيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالفعل لادائماً ای کل انسان متنفس

Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on restricting propositions with essential non-necessity (al-la-darura al-dhatiyya) and essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati). It defines two new types: al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya and al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima. First, it explains that essential non-necessity means the relation is not necessary for the entire existence of the subject, thus implying the possibility of its contradictory. This gives a general possible proposition (mumkina 'amma) differing in quality from the original. Then it defines al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya as the absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) restricted by essential non-necessity, e.g., 'Every human is actually breathing, not necessarily'—meaning it is not necessary that he breathes, but he does at some time. This is composed of an affirmative absolute general and a negative general possible (since 'not necessarily' implies possibility of not breathing). The second type, al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima, is the absolute general restricted by essential non-permanence, e.g., 'No human is actually breathing non-perpetually'—meaning he breathes at some time but not always. The passage then enumerates the possible combinations of four base propositions (the four general types) with four types of restrictions (la-dawam dhati, la-darura dhatiyya, la-dawam wasfi, la-darura wasfiyya) yielding sixteen possibilities. Of these, three are invalid, four are valid and considered (mu'tabara), nine are valid but not considered (ghayr mu'tabara). It also notes that restricting the absolute general by descriptive restrictions is possible but not considered. The author emphasizes that these combinations are not exhaustive; many more are possible for the attentive student to derive.

الوجودية اللاضرورية
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة

Key Takeaway
Al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya is a compound proposition consisting of an absolute general (affirmative) and a general possible (negative), expressing that a predicate holds at some time but is not necessary.

6 min
Definition of al-Wujudiyya al-Āadima
Author defines al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima and explain its restriction by essential non-permanence.

Core Idea
Al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima is the absolute general restricted by essential non-permanence, meaning the predicate holds at some time but not always.

The author defines the second proposition type by restricting the absolute general with essential non-permanence. This type expresses that a predicate occurs at some time but is not perpetual, distinguishing it from permanent propositions.

Guided Path
1
Understand essential non-permanence
Essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati) means the predicate relation is not perpetual for the entire existence of the subject; it occurs at some time but not always.
This is different from descriptive non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-wasfi), which is not considered valid for restricting the two general propositions.
2
Identify the base
The base is again the absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-'amma), which asserts actuality at some time.
3
Apply the restriction
Combining gives al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima: the absolute general restricted by essential non-permanence. The example given: 'No human is actually breathing non-perpetually' — meaning every human breathes at some time, but not perpetually.
The example is a universal negative 'No human is actually breathing non-perpetually', which affirms that every human breathes at some time (affirmative absolute general) and adds that it is not perpetual (negative).
4
Contrast with other restrictions
Note that the same base can also be restricted by essential non-necessity (giving al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya) or by descriptive restrictions, but the latter are not considered valid for the general absolute.
The author later enumerates which combinations are recognized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « باللاضرورة الذاتية»: ومعنى اللاضرورة الذاتية ان هذه النسبة المذكورة في القضية ليست ضرورية مادام ذات الموضوع موجودة فيكون هذا حكماً بامكان نقيضها لان الامكان هو سلب ضرورة الطرف المقابل كما مر فيكون مفاد اللاضرورة الذاتية ممكنة عامة مخالفة للاصل في الكيف . قوله فتسمى الوجودية اللاضرورية»: لان معنى المطلقة العامة هي فعلية النسبة و وجودها فى وقت من الاوقات ولاشتمالها على اللاضرورة، فالوجودية اللاضرورية هى المطلقة العامة المقيدة بالملاضرورة الذاتية نحو كل انسان متنفس بالفعل لا بالضرورة اى لاشيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالامكان العام فهى مركبة من مطلقة عامة و ممكنة عامة احديهما موجبة والاخرى سالبة . قوله «او باللادوام الذاتى»: انما قيد اللادوام بالذاتي، لان تقييد العامتين باللادوام الوصفى غير صحيح ضرورة تنا فى اللادوام بحسب الوصف مع الدوام بحسب الوصف نعم يمكن تقييد الوقتيتين المطلقتين باللادوام الوصفى لكن هذا التركيب غير معتبر عندهم. و اعلم انه كما يصح تقييد هذه القضاياء الاربع باللادوام الذاتي ، كذلك يصح تقييدها باللاضرورية الذاتية و كذلك تقييد ما سوى المشروطة العامة يصح تلك من الجملة باللاضرورة الوصفية فالاحتمالات الحاصلة من ملاحظة كل من تلك القضاياء الاربع مع كل من تلك القيود الاربعة ستة عشر ثلاثة منها غير صحيحة و اربعة منها صحيحة معتبرة و التسعة الباقية صحيحة غير معتبرة. و اعلم ايضاً انه كما يمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللادوام و اللاضرورة الذاتيتين، كذلك يمكن تقييدها باللادوام و اللاضرورة الوصفيتين و هذان ايضاً من الاحتمالات الصحيحة الغير المعتبرة و كما يصح تقييد الممكنة العامة باللاضرورة الذاتية، كذلك يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية وكذا باللادوام الذاتي والوصفى لكن هذه الاحتمالات الثلاثة ايضاً غير معتبرة عندهم. و ينبغى ان يعلم ان التركيب لا ينحصر فما اشرنا اليه بل سيجيء الاشارة الى بعض آخر و يمكن تركيبات كثيرة اخرى لم يتعرضوا لها لكن المتنبه بعد التنبيه بما ذكروه يتمكن من استخراج ای قدر شاء. قوله فتسمى الوجودية اللادائمة»: هى المطلقة العامة المقيدة باللادوام الذاتى نحو لا شيء من الانسان بمتنفس بالفعل لادائماً ای کل انسان متنفس
His saying 'by essential non-necessity': and the meaning of essential non-necessity is that this mentioned relation in the proposition is not necessary as long as the essence of the subject exists, so this is a judgment of the possibility of its contradictory, because possibility is the negation of necessity of the opposite side as has passed, so the content of essential non-necessity is a general possible proposition differing from the original in quality. His saying 'so it is called existential non-necessary': because the meaning of the absolute general is the actuality of the relation and its existence at some time, and it includes non-necessity. So the existential non-necessary is the absolute general restricted by essential non-necessity, like: Every human is actually breathing, not necessarily, i.e., no human is breathing? Actually it's: no human is breathing with general possibility? It is composed of an absolute general and a general possible, one affirmative and the other negative. His saying 'or by essential non-permanence': he only restricted non-permanence by 'essential' because restricting the two general ones by descriptive non-permanence is not valid necessarily? Actually the text is unclear. Yes, it is possible to restrict the two absolute temporals by descriptive non-permanence, but this composition is not considered by them. Know that just as it is valid to restrict these four propositions by essential non-permanence, so too it is valid to restrict them by essential non-necessity, and likewise restricting what other than the general conditional is valid among them by descriptive non-necessity. So the possibilities resulting from considering each of those four propositions with each of those four restrictions are sixteen: three of them are invalid, four of them are valid and considered, and the remaining nine are valid but not considered. Know also that just as it is possible to restrict the absolute general by essential non-permanence and non-necessity, so too it is possible to restrict it by descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity, and these two are also among the valid but not considered possibilities. And just as it is valid to restrict the general possible by essential non-necessity, so too it is valid to restrict it by descriptive non-necessity, and likewise by essential and descriptive non-permanence, but these three possibilities are also not considered by them. It should be known that composition is not limited to what we have indicated, but reference will come to some others, and many other compositions are possible which they did not discuss, but the attentive person, after being alerted by what they mentioned, is able to extract whatever amount he wishes. His saying 'so it is called existential non-perpetual': it is the absolute general restricted by essential non-permanence, like: No human is actually breathing non-perpetually, i.e., every human is breathing? Actually the example: 'No human is actually breathing, not permanently' meaning every human is actually breathing?
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on restricting propositions with essential non-necessity (al-la-darura al-dhatiyya) and essential non-permanence (al-la-dawam al-dhati). It defines two new types: al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya and al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima. First, it explains that essential non-necessity means the relation is not necessary for the entire existence of the subject, thus implying the possibility of its contradictory. This gives a general possible proposition (mumkina 'amma) differing in quality from the original. Then it defines al-wujudiyya al-ladaruriyya as the absolute general (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) restricted by essential non-necessity, e.g., 'Every human is actually breathing, not necessarily'—meaning it is not necessary that he breathes, but he does at some time. This is composed of an affirmative absolute general and a negative general possible (since 'not necessarily' implies possibility of not breathing). The second type, al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima, is the absolute general restricted by essential non-permanence, e.g., 'No human is actually breathing non-perpetually'—meaning he breathes at some time but not always. The passage then enumerates the possible combinations of four base propositions (the four general types) with four types of restrictions (la-dawam dhati, la-darura dhatiyya, la-dawam wasfi, la-darura wasfiyya) yielding sixteen possibilities. Of these, three are invalid, four are valid and considered (mu'tabara), nine are valid but not considered (ghayr mu'tabara). It also notes that restricting the absolute general by descriptive restrictions is possible but not considered. The author emphasizes that these combinations are not exhaustive; many more are possible for the attentive student to derive.

Key Terms
اللادوام الذاتي
الوجودية اللادائمة
المطلقة العامة

Key Takeaway
Al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima is the absolute general proposition restricted by essential non-permanence, expressing that a predicate holds at some time but not perpetually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Formulate a proposition in the form of al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima on a fiqh topic: e.g., 'No prayer is performed without purification non-perpetually' (meaning every prayer requires purification at the time of prayer, but not all the time). Decompose it.
?

Propositions
advanced
12 min
The Sixteen Possible Combinations of Propositions and Restrictions
Author enumerates and classifies possible combinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the sixteen possible combinations of four base propositions with four restrictions and classify them into invalid, valid-and-considered, and valid-but-not-considered categories.

Core Idea
Only 4 of 16 logical combinations are recognized as valid and considered; 9 are valid but not considered, and 3 are invalid.

The author systematically lists all possibilities of restricting the four general propositions by four types of restrictions. This exhaustive approach shows the logical rigor of later logicians and clarifies which compound propositions are accepted in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four base propositions
The four base propositions likely include: absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma), general possible (mumkina 'amma), general conditional (mashruta 'amma), and a fourth (perhaps general perpetual). The exact list is not fully specified, but they are the general types that can be restricted.
The text says 'these four propositions' referring to earlier context.
2
Identify the four restrictions
The four restrictions are: essential non-permanence (la-dawam dhati), essential non-necessity (la-darura dhatiyya), descriptive non-permanence (la-dawam wasfi), and descriptive non-necessity (la-darura wasfiyya).
Two are essential (dhati), two are descriptive (wasfi).
3
Calculate total combinations
4 bases × 4 restrictions = 16 possible combinations.
4
Classify the 16 into three groups
The author states: 3 are invalid, 4 are valid and considered, and 9 are valid but not considered. Invalid combinations are logically inconsistent; valid-and-considered are recognized in the tradition; valid-but-not-considered are logically possible but not used.
Examples: restricting absolute general by descriptive restrictions is valid but not considered.
5
Understand the significance
This classification teaches that logical possibility does not guarantee acceptance; additional criteria (such as utility or convention) determine which propositions are used.
The attentive student can derive further combinations not explicitly listed.

5 min
Classifying the Sixteen Combinations of Propositions and Restrictions
Author categorizes the sixteen combinations of four propositions with four restrictions into invalid, valid-considered, and valid-unconsidered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any of the sixteen combinations of propositions and restrictions as invalid, valid-considered, or valid-unconsidered based on the principles given.

Core Idea
Not every logically valid combination is standard in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic enumeration of possible combinations between four proposition types and four restriction types. Understanding this classification helps you see why only a few proposition forms are actually used in logical analysis, while others remain theoretically possible but practically disregarded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four base propositions and four restrictions
Recall the four general proposition types: mashruta 'amma, 'urfiyya 'amma, waqtiyya mutlaqa, muntashira mutlaqa. The four restrictions are essential non-permanence, essential non-necessity, descriptive non-permanence, descriptive non-necessity. These yield 16 combinations.
The author assumes familiarity with these terms from earlier lessons.
2
Understand the three categories
The author divides the 16 combinations into three groups: invalid (3), valid-considered (4), and valid-unconsidered (9). Invalid likely means logically impossible; valid-unconsidered means logically possible but not standard practice.
The exact counts must be memorized as part of the tradition.
3
Examine additional unconsidered possibilities
The author extends the list by noting that the absolute general can also be restricted by descriptive non-permanence and descriptive non-necessity (2 more), and the general possible by descriptive non-necessity, essential non-permanence, descriptive non-permanence (3 more). All these are valid but unconsidered.
These extra possibilities are not part of the original 16 but are extensions.
4
Recognize the open-ended nature
The author states that more compositions exist beyond those mentioned. The attentive student can derive further possibilities from the principles. This highlights the difference between theoretical logical space and the selected standard forms.
The tradition focuses on a limited set for practical use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالاحتمالات الحاصلة من ملاحظة كل من تلك القضاياء الاربع مع كل من تلك القيود الاربعة ستة عشر ثلاثة منها غير صحيحة و اربعة منها صحيحة معتبرة و التسعة الباقية صحيحة غير معتبرة. و اعلم ايضاً انه كما يمكن تقييد المطلقة العامة باللادوام و اللاضرورة الذاتيتين، كذلك يمكن تقييدها باللادوام و اللاضرورة الوصفيتين و هذان ايضاً من الاحتمالات الصحيحة الغير المعتبرة و كما يصح تقييد الممكنة العامة باللاضرورة الذاتية، كذلك يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية وكذا باللادوام الذاتي والوصفى لكن هذه الاحتمالات الثلاثة ايضاً غير معتبرة عندهم. و ينبغى ان يعلم ان التركيب لا ينحصر فما اشرنا اليه بل سيجيء الاشارة الى بعض آخر و يمكن تركيبات كثيرة اخرى لم يتعرضوا لها لكن المتنبه بعد التنبيه بما ذكروه يتمكن من استخراج ای قدر شاء.
So the possibilities resulting from considering each of those four propositions with each of those four restrictions are sixteen: three of them are invalid, four of them are valid and considered, and the remaining nine are valid but not considered. Know also that just as it is possible to restrict the absolute general by essential non-permanence and non-necessity, so too it is possible to restrict it by descriptive non-permanence and non-necessity, and these two are also among the valid but not considered possibilities. And just as it is valid to restrict the general possible by essential non-necessity, so too it is valid to restrict it by descriptive non-necessity, and likewise by essential and descriptive non-permanence, but these three possibilities are also not considered by them. And it should be known that composition is not limited to what we have indicated, but reference will come to some others, and many other compositions are possible which they did not discuss, but the attentive person, after being alerted by what they mentioned, is able to extract whatever amount he wishes.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and classification of the possible combinations of four base propositions (the four general types: mashruta 'amma, 'urfiyya 'amma, waqtiyya mutlaqa, muntashira mutlaqa) with four types of restrictions (essential non-permanence, essential non-necessity, descriptive non-permanence, descriptive non-necessity). The author states that there are 16 possible combinations. Among them, three are invalid (likely contradictory or impossible), four are valid and considered by scholars (mu'tabara), and the remaining nine are logically valid but not considered in practice (ghayr mu'tabara). The author then gives additional specific examples: restricting the absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma) by descriptive non-permanence and descriptive non-necessity yields two more valid but unconsidered possibilities. Similarly, restricting the general possible (mumkina 'amma) by descriptive non-necessity, essential non-permanence, and descriptive non-permanence yields three more unconsidered possibilities. This totals 4 considered + 9 unconsidered + 3 invalid = 16, though the specific counts need cross-checking. The author concludes by noting that the topic is open-ended; many more compositions are possible beyond these sixteen, but the attentive student can derive them from the principles given.

D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
From the 16 possible combinations of propositions and restrictions, only 4 are standard; the rest are either invalid or not traditionally used.

The Seven Valid Compound Modal Propositions
Enumeration of seven valid compound modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven valid compound modal propositions and identify which modality (lā-dawām or lā-ḍarūrah) each contains.

Core Idea
Only seven compound modal propositions are valid; they combine absolute and possible propositions.

In this lesson, we explore the classification of compound modal propositions in Islamic logic. The text enumerates exactly seven that are considered valid, each composed of a general absolute and a general possible proposition. Understanding this inventory is crucial for constructing valid modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven names
The author lists seven: al-mashrūṭah al-khāṣṣah (special conditional), al-ʿurfiyyah al-khāṣṣah (special conventional), al-waqtiyyah (temporal), al-muntashirah (extended), al-wujūdiyyah al-lā-ḍarūriyyah (existential non-necessary), al-wujūdiyyah al-lā-dāʾimah (existential non-permanent), and al-mumkinah al-khāṣṣah (special possible).
These are technical terms; learn their Arabic names.
2
Identify the two underlying modalities
The first four and the existential non-permanent use lā-dawām (non-permanence), indicating a general absolute proposition. The existential non-necessary and the special possible use lā-ḍarūrah (non-necessity), indicating a general possible proposition.
lā-dawām points to absolute (actual) existence; lā-ḍarūrah points to possibility (non-necessity).
3
Understand the composition rule
Each compound has two parts differing in quality (one affirmative, one negative) but agreeing in quantity (both universal or both particular). The subject is the same.
For example, 'Every S is P absolutely and no S is P necessarily' is a compound.
4
Apply to a sample
Take 'Everything created is temporal (absolute) and it is not necessary that it is temporal (possible)'. This is an existential non-necessary compound (lā-ḍarūrah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وهذه «مركبات» أي هذه القضايا السبع المذكورة وهي: المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة والوقتية والمنتشرة والوجودية اللاضرورية والوجودية اللادائمة والممكنة الخاصة لأن اللادوام في الأربع الأولى وفي الوجودية اللادائمة إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة واللاضرورة في الوجودية اللاضرورية وفي الممكنة الخاصة إشارة إلى ممكنة عامة. قوله «مخالفتي الكيفية» أي في الإيجاب والسلب وقد مر بيان ذلك في بيان معنى اللادوام واللاضرورة وأما الموافقة في الكمية أي الكلية والجزئية، فلأن الموضوع في القضية المركبة أمر واحد وقد حكم عليه بحكمين مختلفين بالإيجاب والسلب فإن كان الحكم في الجزء الأول على كل أفراده كان الحكم في الجزء الثاني أيضاً على كل أفراده.
His saying: 'And these are compound' means these seven mentioned propositions, which are: the special conditional, the special conventional, the temporal, the extended, the existential non-necessary, the existential non-permanent, and the special possible. Because non-permanence in the first four and in the existential non-permanent indicates a general absolute proposition, and non-necessity in the existential non-necessary and in the special possible indicates a general possible proposition. His saying: 'Differing in quality' means in affirmation and negation, and the explanation of that has already passed in the explanation of the meaning of non-permanence and non-necessity. As for agreement in quantity, i.e., universality and particularity, it is because the subject in the compound proposition is one thing, and two different judgments of affirmation and negation are made upon it. If the judgment in the first part applies to all individuals, then the judgment in the second part also applies to all individuals.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an explanation from the hashīyah (super-commentary) on the matn's statement that certain propositions are 'compound' (murakkabāt). The author enumerates the seven valid compound modal propositions: (1) al-mashrūṭah al-khāṣṣah (special conditional), (2) al-ʿurfiyyah al-khāṣṣah (special conventional), (3) al-waqtiyyah (temporal), (4) al-muntashirah (extended), (5) al-wujūdiyyah al-lā-ḍarūriyyah (existential non-necessary), (6) al-wujūdiyyah al-lā-dāʾimah (existential non-permanent), and (7) al-mumkinah al-khāṣṣah (special possible). He then explains the logical composition of these seven: The first four (al-mashrūṭah al-khāṣṣah etc.) and the existential non-permanent contain the modality of 'non-permanence' (al-lā-dawām), which indicates an underlying general absolute proposition (al-muṭlaqah al-ʿāmmah). The existential non-necessary and the special possible contain 'non-necessity' (al-lā-ḍarūrah), which indicates an underlying general possible proposition (al-mumkinah al-ʿāmmah). Thus, each compound proposition is composed of two simpler propositions: one absolute (affirmative or negative) and one possible (negative or affirmative), differing in quality (one affirmative, one negative) but agreeing in quantity (both universal or both particular). For example, the existential non-necessary means 'Everything that is S is P actually (absolute) and not necessarily (possible negative)'. The author stresses that the subject is the same in both component propositions, so the quantity (universality or particularity) must match. This detailed classification is crucial for understanding modal logic in the Islamic tradition, particularly in syllogistic arguments where the modality of premises affects the conclusion. Qur'anic examples: 'All humans are mortal necessarily' (general absolute) vs. 'No human is immortal necessarily' (possible negative) combine to form a compound conditional. Fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful (absolute), and no intoxicant is lawful (possible)' would be a compound of two opposites. The seven propositions listed are considered the standard inventory of valid compound modal statements in the Aristotelian logical tradition as taught by later Muslim logicians.

Key Takeaway
The seven compound modal propositions are the standard valid combinations of absolute and possible modalities in post-Avicennan logic.
