The Structure of Compound Propositions
Explains the composition of the seven compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven compound propositions and explain how they are formed from a general absolute and a general possible proposition.

Core Idea
Compound propositions combine a general absolute and a general possible that differ in quality but agree in quantity.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the seven compound propositions are not simple but are composed of two underlying general propositions: a general absolute (indicated by 'non-permanence') and a general possible (indicated by 'non-necessity'). These two components must have opposite quality (one affirmative, the other negative) but the same quantity (both universal or both particular). Understanding this structure is key to correctly analysing modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
Look for 'non-permanence' (al-ladawam) and 'non-necessity' (al-darura) in the proposition; these indicate a general absolute and a general possible, respectively.
2
Check quality difference
The two parts must differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. For example, a compound proposition cannot have both parts affirmative; one part must be general absolute (affirmative) and the other general possible (negative) or vice versa.
3
Check quantity agreement
Both parts must have the same quantity: either both universal (covering all individuals) or both particular (covering some). This ensures the subject term is the same and the judgments are complete.
4
Recognize the seven types
The seven compound propositions are: the particular conditioned, the particular customary, the temporal, the widespread, the non-necessary existential, the non-permanent existential, and the particular possible. Each is a compound of a general absolute and a general possible.
The passage lists these seven types explicitly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ايضاً فتسمى الممكنة الخاصة و هذه مركبات لان اللادوام اشارة الى مطلقة عامة واللاضرورة الى ممكنة عامة مخالفتى الكيفية موافقتى الكمية. قوله وهذه «مركبات» اى هذه القضايا السبع المذكورة وهي: المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة والوقتية والمنتشرة والوجودية اللاضرورية والوجودية اللادائمة والممكنة الخاصة لان اللادوام في الاربع الأولى وفي الموجودية اللادائمة اشارة الى مطلقة عامة واللاضرورة في الوجودية اللاضرورية وفي الممكنة الخاصة اشارة الى ممكنة عامة. قوله «مخالفتى الكيفية»: أى في الايجاب والسلب وقد مر بيان ذلك في بيان معنى اللادوام واللاضرورة واما الموافقة في الكمية اى الكلية والجزئية، فلان الموضوع في القضية المركبة امر واحد وقد حكم عليه بحكمين مختلفين بالايجاب والسلب فان كان الحكم في الجزء الأول على كل افراده كان الحكم في الجزء الثاني ايضاً على كلها وان كان على البعض في الأول فكذا في الثاني. قوله « لما قيد بها» اى القضية التي قيدت بها باللادوام واللاضرورة يعني اصل القضية.
Also, the particular possible is named, and these are compound because non-permanence indicates a general absolute, and non-necessity indicates a general possible, differing in quality and agreeing in quantity. His statement 'and these are compound' means these mentioned seven propositions, which are: the particular conditioned, the particular customary, the temporal, the widespread, the non-necessary existential, the non-permanent existential, and the particular possible, because non-permanence in the first four and in the non-permanent existential indicates a general absolute, and non-necessity in the non-necessary existential and in the particular possible indicates a general possible. His statement 'differing in quality' means in affirmation and negation, and its explanation has already passed in the explanation of the meaning of non-permanence and non-necessity. As for agreement in quantity, i.e., universality and particularity, because the subject in the compound proposition is one thing, and two different judgments of affirmation and negation are made about it. So if the judgment in the first part is on all its individuals, then the judgment in the second part is also on all of them; and if it is on some in the first, then likewise in the second. His statement 'when it is qualified by them' means the proposition that is qualified by non-permanence and non-necessity, i.e., the original proposition.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the seven compound propositions. The commentator explains that the particular possible (al-mumkina al-khassa) is compound because it contains both a general absolute (muttlaqa 'amma) indicated by 'non-permanence' (al-ladawam) and a general possible (mumkina 'amma) indicated by 'non-necessity' (al-darura). The seven compound propositions are listed: the particular conditioned, the particular customary, the temporal, the widespread, the non-necessary existential, the non-permanent existential, and the particular possible. Each of these compounds because either 'non-permanence' or 'non-necessity' points to a general proposition. The commentator then clarifies that 'differing in quality' refers to affirmation and negation, while 'agreeing in quantity' means both parts have the same universality or particularity. This is because the subject is one, and two opposite judgments are made: if the first judgment covers all individuals, the second also covers all; if only some, then both are partial. Finally, the phrase 'when qualified by them' refers to the original proposition modified by non-permanence and non-necessity.

Types of Conditional Propositions
Author defines the two types of conditional propositions and subdivides muttasila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muttasila and munfasila conditional propositions and subdivide muttasila into lazimiyya and ittifaqiyya based on the presence of a logical relation.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are either connected or disjoined; the connected type is either implicative or coincidental.

In this passage, the author begins the formal division of conditional propositions. He defines the two main categories—connected (muttasila) and disjoined (munfasila)—and subdivides the connected type into implicative (lazimiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya). He also addresses a methodological objection about the need to define before dividing, clarifying that a general definition suffices for division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines the division of conditional propositions. The two main types are muttasila (connected) and munfasila (disjoined).
2
Extract the defining feature of muttasila
A muttasila conditional judges a relation (thubūt nisba) on the condition of another proposition (taqdīr ukhrā). It follows an 'if...then' structure.
3
Subdivide muttasila into lazimiyya and ittifaqiyya
If the connection is due to a logical relation (ʿalāqa), it is lazimiyya (implicative). If merely coincidental, it is ittifaqiyya.
The 'alāqa ensures necessary consequence; without it, the connection is accidental.
4
Note the incomplete definition of munfasila
The text begins the definition of munfasila ('and disjoined if a judgment is made in it...') but cuts off. From the context, munfasila involves a disjunction (either...or).
The full definition appears on subsequent pages.
5
Understand the objection and response
An objector claims one must first define 'conditional' before dividing it. The hashya responds that a general definition—drawn from the earlier statement contrasting categorical and conditional—is sufficient for the purpose of division.
This is a methodological point about the order of exposition vs. the order of learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: الشرطية متصلة ان حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير اخرى او بنفيها. لزومية ان كان ذلك لعلاقة والافاتفاقية. ومنفصلة ان حكم فيها. أورد عليه بان الشيء يجب ان يعرف أولاً ثم يقسم، فان معرفة اقسام الشيء فرع معرفته فقبل تعريف الشرطية لايصح تقسيمه. واجيب بان المعرفة الاجمالية تكفى فى مقام التقسيم والمصنف حيث قال فيما تقدم: «ان القضية ان حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه فحملية والافشرطية»، علم منه تعريف اجمالى للشرطية بانها عالم يحكم فيه بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه، وهذا القدر يكفى فى مقام التقسيم.
Chapter: The conditional proposition is connected if a judgment is made in it of the affirmation of a relation on the condition of another or its negation. Lazimiyya if that is due to a relation, otherwise ittfiaqiyya. And disjoined if a judgment is made in it... An objection was raised against it: A thing must first be known, then divided, for knowledge of the parts of a thing is a branch of knowing it, so before defining the conditional it is not valid to divide it. And it was answered: General knowledge suffices for the purpose of division, and the author said in what preceded: 'The proposition: if a judgment is made in it of affirming something of something or negating it, then it is categorical, otherwise conditional.' From that, a general definition of the conditional is known: it is a matter in which a judgment is made of affirming something of something or negating it, and this amount suffices for the purpose of division.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the division of conditional propositions. A conditional is 'connected' (muttasila) if it judges a relation between two propositions on a condition; 'disjoined' (munfasila) if it judges something else (the text is cut off). Muttasila is further divided into 'lazimiyya' (implicative) if the connection is due to a relation (ʿalāqa), and 'ittifaqiyya' (coincidental) if not. The commentator then addresses an objection: one must define something before dividing it, but the author has only given an implicit definition of the conditional as 'a proposition that is not categorical'. The response is that a general definition suffices for division, and that the author's earlier statement—'if it affirms or negates something of something, it is categorical; otherwise, conditional'—provides enough general knowledge for division.

Classification of Connected Conditionals: Luzumiyya and Ittifaqiyya
Definition and division of muttasila into luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya connected conditionals by identifying whether a relation (ʿalāqa) is present.

Core Idea
A connected conditional is either implicative (luzumiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya), based on the presence of a real relation.

In this passage, the author defines the connected conditional (muttasila) and divides it into two types: luzumiyya, where the connection is due to a relation like causality, and ittifaqiyya, where the connection is merely coincidental. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the strength of conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine whether the passage presents a muttasila. Look for 'if...then' structure.
2
Check for a relation (ʿalāqa)
Ask whether the antecedent entails the consequent due to a real connection (e.g., causality) or mere coincidence.
3
Classify as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya
If there is a relation, it is luzumiyya; if not, ittifaqiyya.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Use the example given: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' – there is no relation, so it is ittifaqiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: الشرطية متصلة ان حكم فيها بثبوت نسبة على تقدير اخرى او بنفيها. اللزومية ان كان ذلك لعلاقة والا فاتفاقية. فالمتصلة الموجبة ما حكم فيها باتصال النسبتين والسالبة ما حكم فيها بسلب اتصالهما. واللزومية الموجبة ما حكم فيها بان الاتصال لعلاقة والسالبة ما حكم فيها بانه ليس هناك اتصال لعلاقة سواء لم يكن هناك اتصال او كان لكن لا لعلاقة. والاتفاقية ما حكم فيها بمجرد الاتصال او نفيه من غير ان يكون ذلك مستندا إلى العلاقة. نحو كلما كان الانسان ناطقا فالحمار ناهق او ليس كلما كان الانسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا. قوله «العلاقة» وهي أمر بسببه يستصحب المقدم التالي كعلية طلوع الشمس لوجود النهار. (0) أورد عليه بان الشيء يجب ان يعرف اولا ثم يقسم. واجيب بان المعرفة الاجمالية تكفي في مقام التقسيم. (ه) انما سميت بها لاشتمالها على لزوم التالي للمقدم. فان قيل ان الاتفاقية أيضا كذلك. قلنا قد سبق مرارا ان المناسبة في التسمية لا يجب اطرادها.
Section: The conditional is connected if judgment is made of the affirmation of a relationship on condition of another, or its negation. The implicative (luzumiyya) is if that is due to a relation, otherwise it is accidental (ittifaqiyya). The affirmative connected is that in which the connection of the two propositions is judged, and the negative is that in which the denial of their connection is judged. The affirmative implicative is that in which the connection is judged to be due to a relation, and the negative is that in which it is judged that there is no connection due to a relation, whether there is no connection or there is but not due to a relation. The accidental is that in which mere connection or its negation is judged without that being based on a relation, like 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' or 'It is not the case that whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying.' His statement 'the relation': it is a thing by virtue of which the antecedent entails the consequent, like the causality of sunrise for the existence of daytime. (0) An objection was raised that a thing must first be known then divided. The answer is that summary knowledge suffices for division. (h) They are named thus because they include the implication of the consequent by the antecedent. If it is said that the accidental also has implication, we say that appropriateness in naming need not be universal.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines and divides the connected conditional (muttasila). It begins by stating that a conditional is connected if a judgment is made that one proposition is true (or false) conditionally upon another. It then splits into two types: luzumiyya (implicative) where the connection is due to a relation (causality or entailment), and ittifaqiyya (accidental) where the connection is coincidental. The text then clarifies the affirmative and negative forms of the connected conditional overall, and then the affirmative and negative forms of the implicative specifically. The accidental is defined as a connection without a relation, with an example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' – there is no real relation. The commentator glosses 'relation' as that which causes the antecedent to entail the consequent, like sunrise causing daytime. A footnote addresses an objection: one must know a thing before dividing it; the answer is that a summary definition suffices for division. Another footnote explains why the implicative is named thus: because it entails the consequent, and addresses the potential objection that accidental also entails; the response is that naming need not cover all cases.

لزومية
اتفاقية
علاقة

B) The presence of a relation (ʿalāqa) between antecedent and consequent
C) The truth value of the antecedent
D) The number of propositions

Key Takeaway
A connected conditional is luzumiyya if the connection is due to a real relation; otherwise it is ittifaqiyya.

The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author defines munfasila and divides it into three types based on incompatibility scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) based on the scope of incompatibility and provide an example of each from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Disjunctives differ by whether they prevent joint truth, joint falsity, or both.

The passage defines the disjunctive conditional (munfasila) as a proposition that judges incompatibility or its absence between two propositions. It then divides the affirmative disjunctive into three types according to the nature of that incompatibility. This classification is crucial for understanding the truth conditions of disjunctive statements used in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a disjunctive conditional
A disjunctive conditional (munfasila) is a proposition that judges either incompatibility (tanāfī) or non-incompatibility between two component propositions. This judgment can be affirmative (asserting incompatibility) or negative (denying it).
2
Examine the three types of affirmative disjunctives
When the disjunctive is affirmative, the incompatibility can be in three ways: (1) in both truth and falsity (haqiqiyya), (2) in truth only (mani'at al-jam'), or (3) in falsity only (mani'at al-khuluw). Each type has distinct truth conditions.
The passage gives an example for haqiqiyya: 'Either this number is even or it is odd.' For mani'at al-jam', an example could be 'Either it is a plant or an animal' (cannot both be true, but can both be false). For mani'at al-khuluw, an example is 'Either it is a cat or it is a mammal' (cannot both be false, but can both be true).
3
Differentiate the types using truth tables
For haqiqiyya: exactly one is true. For mani'at al-jam': at least one is false (cannot both be true). For mani'at al-khuluw: at least one is true (cannot both be false). Note that haqiqiyya implies both others, but not vice versa.
4
Apply the classification to a real Islamic example
Consider the disjunctive from usul al-fiqh: 'Either this ruling is wajib or it is mubah.' If the ruling must be either wajib or mubah but cannot be both or neither when considering all possibilities, it is haqiqiyya. In a context where the two are mutually exclusive but both could be false (if another category applies), it could be mani'at al-jam'.
This illustrates how the classification guides juristic reasoning.

والمنفصلة ان حكم فيها بتنا في النسبتين او لا تنا فيهما. وهي الحقيقية ان كان التنافي في الصدق والكذب، او صدقا فقط فمانعة الجمع، او كذبا فقط فمانعة الخلو. وكل منها عنادية ان كان التنافي لذاتيهما والا فاتفاقية. فالمنفصلة الحقيقية ما حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين في الصدق والكذب كقولنا اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجا واما ان يكون فردا. والمانعة الجمع ما حكم فيها بتنافي النسبتين اولا تنافيهما في الصدق فقط. قوله «بتنا في النسبتين»: سواء كانت النسبتان ثبوتيتين او سلبيتين او مختلفتين. فان كان الحكم فيها بتنا فيها فهي منفصلة موجبة، وان كان يسلب تنا فيهما فهي منفصلة سالبة. (٥) قوله: «بتنا في النسبتين...» فان قيل ان المنفصلة قد تشتمل على ثلاثة اجزاء كقولهم: الكلمة اما اسم او فعل او حرف. قلنا: لانعلم ان هذا القول منفصلة بل حملية مرددة المحمول، ولو سلم فنقول ان التعريف المذكور للمنفصلة المعتبرة في العلوم الحكمية، ولو سلم فنقول ان هذا في الحقيقة منفصلتان مشتملتان على جزئين لا واحدة مشتملة على ثلاثة اجزاء.
And the disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of incompatibility between the two propositions or their non-incompatibility. It is the real (haqiqiyya) if the incompatibility is in truth and falsity, or only in truth then it is preventing conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), or only in falsity then it is preventing void (mani'at al-khuluw). And each of them is adversative (ʿinadiyya) if the incompatibility is due to their essences, otherwise it is accidental. The real disjunctive is that in which the incompatibility of the two propositions in truth and falsity is judged, like 'Either this number is even or it is odd.' The preventing conjunction is that in which the incompatibility of the two propositions is judged, or their non-incompatibility in truth only. His statement 'incompatibility of the two propositions': whether the two propositions are affirmative, negative, or different. If judgment is made of incompatibility between them, it is an affirmative disjunctive, and if it negates incompatibility between them, it is a negative disjunctive. (5) His statement 'incompatibility of the two propositions...' - If it is said that the disjunctive may contain three parts, like 'The word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle,' we say: we do not accept that this statement is a disjunctive; rather it is a categorical with a disjunctive predicate. And if we accept, we say: the aforementioned definition is for the disjunctive considered in the philosophical sciences. And if we accept, we say: this is in reality two disjunctives each comprising two parts, not one comprising three parts.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines and divides the disjunctive conditional (munfasila). It begins by stating that a conditional is disjunctive if it judges incompatibility (tanāfī) or its absence between two propositions. The main division is based on the scope of incompatibility: (1) Haqiqiyya (real): incompatibility in both truth and falsity, e.g., 'Either this number is even or odd' – they cannot both be true or both false. (2) Mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction): incompatibility only in truth – they cannot both be true but can both be false, e.g., 'Either it is a plant or an animal' – false if both true, but it could be neither. (3) Mani'at al-khuluw (preventing void): incompatibility only in falsity – they cannot both be false but can both be true. The passage also notes a sub-division: each of these can be 'inadiyya (adversative) if the incompatibility is due to the essences of the two propositions, otherwise it is ittifaqiyya (accidental). The text then clarifies the general definition of munfasila: it can be affirmative (judging incompatibility) or negative (denying incompatibility). A lengthy footnote addresses an objection: how can a disjunctive have three parts (e.g., 'word is noun, verb, or particle') when the definition mentions two propositions? Several answers are given: it is actually a categorical with a disjunctive predicate; the definition applies to philosophical logic, not grammar; it reduces to two binary disjunctives; etc.

Essential vs. Accidental Incompatibility and the Three-Part Challenge
Author discusses 'inadiyya/ittifaqiyya subdivision and responds to objection against binary nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between 'inadiyya and ittifaqiyya disjunctives, and evaluate the responses to the objection that some disjunctives have three parts.

Core Idea
Incompatibility can be essential (due to the natures of the propositions) or accidental, and disjunctives are always binary in formal logic.

After classifying disjunctives by scope, the passage adds a further subdivision: the incompatibility may be essential ('inadiyya) or accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author then addresses an objection about three-part disjunctives, defending the binary nature of the formal disjunctive. This lesson clarifies these subtle points and their logical significance.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'inadiyya and ittifaqiyya disjunctives
A disjunctive is 'inadiyya (adversative) when the incompatibility between the two propositions is due to their very essences – they are opposites by nature. For example, 'Either this number is even or odd' is 'inadiyya because even and odd are essential opposites. If the incompatibility is not essential but accidental, it is ittifaqiyya, e.g., 'Either Zayd is in the house or Amr is in the garden' – these are not inherently opposed.
2
Analyze the three-part objection
The objection states that some disjunctives have three parts, like 'The word is either a noun, a verb, or a particle.' This seems to contradict the definition that disjunctives involve two propositions. Understand the objection and why it is problematic for the definition.
3
Evaluate the three responses
The author gives three responses: (1) Such statements are not true disjunctives but categoricals with disjunctive predicates; (2) The definition applies specifically to the disjunctives used in philosophical sciences (not grammar); (3) It is actually two binary disjunctives combined. Assess each response's strength.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider a fiqh statement: 'The beverage is either wine, grape juice, or vinegar.' Is this a single disjunctive or multiple? Use the author's logic to analyze it as a series of binary choices.

Types of Disjunctive Propositions: Haqiqiyya, Mani'at al-Jam', Mani'at al-Khuluw
Author defines three types of disjunctive propositions based on truth-value combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive propositions based on their truth-value conditions and identify which type is used in a given example from Islamic theological or legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Disjunctives are defined by whether their parts oppose in truth, falsehood, or both.

This passage classifies disjunctive propositions (munfasila) into three fundamental types according to how the truth values of their two parts relate. Understanding this classification is essential for constructing and analyzing compound arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where disjunctions often appear in conditional rulings and theological distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
The author first states that disjunctions can be positive (affirming opposition) or negative (denying opposition). All three types defined here are positive disjunctions. What is the common genus? They all affirm opposition between the two parts.
Negative disjunctions are set aside for this discussion.
2
Extract the differentia for each type
For haqiqiyya: opposition in both truth and falsehood (cannot be both true nor both false). For mani'at al-jam': opposition only in truth (cannot both be true, but may both be false). For mani'at al-khuluw: opposition only in falsehood (cannot both be false, but may both be true).
3
Verify with the given examples
The number example (even/odd) fits haqiqiyya: a number cannot be both even and odd, nor neither. The stone/tree example fits mani'at al-jam': can be neither (e.g., water). The Zayd/sea example fits mani'at al-khuluw: if he is not in the sea, he does not drown (so at least one true); they can both be false? No, they cannot both be false.
Check if the examples satisfy the conditions strictly.
4
Understand narrower vs broader senses
For mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw, the author distinguishes narrower (considering only the specific opposition, e.g., only impossibility of both true) and broader (considering the condition without regard to the other dimension) senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «بتنافى النسبتين»: سواء كان النسبتان ثبوتيتين أو سلبيتين أو مختلفتين، فإن كان الحكم فيها بتنافيهما فهي منفصلة موجبة، وإن كان يسلب التنافي فيهما فهي منفصلة سالبة. ثم قال: «وهي: الحقيقية». فالمنفصلة الحقيقية ما حكم فيها بتنافى النسبتين في الصدق والكذب، كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً وإما أن يكون هذا العدد فرداً، أو حكم فيها بسلب التنافي في النسبتين في الصدق والكذب نحو قولنا: ليس البتة إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو منقسماً بمتساويين. والمنفصلة المانعة الجمع ما حكم فيها بتنافى النسبتين أو لا تنافيهما في الصدق فقط مع جواز اجتماعهما في الكذب، كقولك: هذا الشيء إما أن يكون حجراً وإما أن يكون شجراً. والمنفصلة المانعة الخلو ما حكم فيها بتنافى النسبتين أو لا تنافيهما في الكذب فقط مع جواز اجتماعهما في الصدق، كقولك: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق. قوله «أو صدقاً فقط» أي لا في الكذب أو مع قطع النظر عن الكذب، حتى جاز أن يجتمع النسبتان في الكذب وأن لا يجتمعا، ويقال للمعنى الأول مانعة الجمع بالمعنى الأخص، وللثاني مانعة الجمع بالمعنى الأعم. قوله «أو كذباً فقط» أي لا في الصدق أو مع قطع النظر عن الصدق، والأول مانعة الخلو بالمعنى الأخص والثاني بالمعنى الأعم.
His statement: 'Regarding the opposition of the two relations': Whether the two relations are affirmative or negative or different, so if the judgment in them is of opposition, it is a positive disjunctive, and if opposition is negated in them, it is a negative disjunctive. Then he said: 'And it is the true one.' So the true disjunctive is that in which judgment is made of the opposition of the two relations in truth and falsehood, as in our saying: 'Either this number is even or this number is odd', or judgment is made negating opposition in the two relations in truth and falsehood, as in our saying: 'Absolutely not; either this number is even or divisible into two equal parts.' The exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam') is that in which judgment is made of the opposition of the two relations or their non-opposition only in truth, with the permissibility of their conjunction in falsehood, as in your saying: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree.' The inclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-khuluw) is that in which judgment is made of the opposition of the two relations or their non-opposition only in falsehood, with the permissibility of their conjunction in truth, as in your saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown.' His statement: 'or only in truth' means not in falsehood, or disregarding falsehood, such that it is permissible for the two relations to be together in falsehood or not to be together; the first meaning is called exclusive disjunction in the narrower sense, and the second in the broader sense. His statement: 'or only in falsehood' means not in truth, or disregarding truth; the first is inclusive disjunction in the narrower sense and the second in the broader sense.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines three primary types of disjunctive propositions (munfasila) based on the truth-value relations between their two parts. First, the commentator clarifies that a disjunction can be positive (affirming opposition between the parts) or negative (denying opposition). Then, three specific types are defined: (1) True disjunction (haqiqiyya): the two parts cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false; they oppose each other in both truth and falsehood. For example, a number is either even or odd — they cannot both be true or both false. (2) Exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam'): the two parts cannot be true together but may be false together. The commentator gives the example: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree' — it cannot be both, but it could be neither (e.g., a liquid). (3) Inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw): the two parts cannot be false together but may be true together. The example: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' — if he is not in the sea, he does not drown (true), so they cannot both be false; but if he is in the sea, he drowns (so the first true, second false), so they are not both true. The commentator then refines these definitions: 'exclusive disjunction in the narrower sense' considers only the condition of not being both true, disregarding falsehood; 'exclusive in the broader sense' allows the condition to be considered with or without falsehood. Similarly for inclusive. This classification is fundamental for analyzing compound statements in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية
المانعة الجمع
المانعة الخلو
بالمعنى الأخص
بالمعنى الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Mani'at al-jam'
C) Mani'at al-khuluw
D) Salba

Key Takeaway
Disjunctions are classified by whether their parts oppose in truth only, falsehood only, or both, and this classification is foundational for analyzing compound arguments in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
2. Consider the statement: 'Either this water is pure or it is impure.' If we know water can be pure or impure but also could be something else (like doubtful), what type of disjunction might this be? Identify using the definitions from the passage.

Disjunctive Conditionals: Man'at al-Jam' and Man'at al-Khuluw
Defines and subdivides disjunctive conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw and identify their specific and general forms in Islamic legal arguments.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals split into two kinds: one prevents joint truth, the other prevents joint falsehood.

In this lesson, we explore the two main types of disjunctive conditionals defined by the logicians: man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw. These distinctions are crucial for evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam, where legal or theological positions are often presented as mutually exclusive alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Read the Arabic definition of man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw. Man'at al-jam' prevents the two parts from being true together. Man'at al-khuluw prevents them from being false together.
Pay attention to the Arabic terms: مانعة الجمع means 'preventing combination' and مانعة الخلو means 'preventing emptiness'.
2
Understand the subdivisions
Each type has a specific (akhass) and a general (a'am) sense. For man'at al-jam', the specific sense means both cannot be true (they may both be false), while the general sense means they are not true together but could both be false. For man'at al-khuluw, the specific sense means both cannot be false (they may both be true), while the general sense means they are not false together but could both be true.
The commentary explains these using 'his saying "or only truth" and "or only falsehood" '.
3
Test with examples from the text
The example 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown' is given as man'at al-khuluw. Analyze why: if Zayd is not in the sea, he drowns, so falsehood cannot happen together. Now think of an example of man'at al-jam' from Islamic law: 'This action is either obligatory or prohibited' – they cannot be true together.
Verify that the two sides in man'at al-jam' can both be false (e.g., the action could be recommended, not obligatory or prohibited).
4
Apply to a real Islamic science context
In usul al-fiqh, a mujtahid might say: 'The ruling is either haram or halal.' This is a disjunctive conditional. Determine which type it is and which subdivision applies.
Consider whether the two can be both false (neither haram nor halal) – that would make it man'at al-jam' bi al-ma'na al-akhass if they cannot both be true, but if there is a third option like mubah, then it might be man'at al-jam' bi al-ma'na al-akhass? Actually, if there are more than two options, the disjunction may not be exhaustive. The logicians assume two-part disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام الشرطية لذاتي الجزئين والافاتفاقية. ثم الحكم في الشرطية ان كان على جميع تقادير المقدم نحو هذا الشيء اما ان يكون حجراً و اما ان يكون شجراً (۱۷) والمنفصلة المانعة الخلو (۱۸) ما حكم فيها بتنافى النسبتين اولاتنا فيهما في الكذب فقط كقولك : اما ان يكون زيد فى البحر و اما ان لا يغرق (۱۹). :قوله او صدقاً فقط» اى لا فى الكذب او مع قطع النظر عن الكذب (۲۰) حتى جاز ان يجتمع النسبتان فى الكذب و ان لا يجتمعا ويقال للمعنى الاول مانعة الجمع بالمعنى الاخص (۲۱) و للثانى مانعة الجمع بالمعنى الاعم. :قوله «اوكذباً فقط» اى: لا في الصدق او مع قطع النظر عن الصدق، و الاول مانعة الخلو بالمعنى الاخص و الثاني بالمعنى الاعم. (ه) اى ان كان الحكم باللزوم والعناد في الشرطية على جميع التقادير والأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم و ان كانت محالة فى انفسها، فالقضية كلية كقولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وكقولنا هذا العدد اما ان يكون زوجاً او فرداً فان الحكم باللزوم في الاول و بالعناد في الثاني على جميع التقادير والاوضاع الممكنة بمعنى ان وجود النهار لازم لطلوع الشمس في جميع حالاتها وازمانها و اوضاعها من كونها منكسفة او غير منكسفة محجوبة بغيم او غير محجوبة و من كونها في الحمل او السرطان أو غيرهما ومن كونها مع نطق زيد او ضرب عمرو او غير ذلك مما لا تعد ولا تحصى و هكذا عناد الفردية المزوجية. ثم انما اعتبرنا امكان الاجتماع لانه لواطلق لم يصدق شرطية كلية لا متصلة ولا منفصلة. اما في الأولى فلان من التقادير ما لا يلزم معه التالي ككون المقدم مع عدم التالى مثلاً فح لا يصح الحكم بلزوم التالي على جميع تقادير المقدم والالزم اجتماع النقيضين. و اما في الثانية فلان من التقادير ما لا يعاند معه التالى المقدم ككون المقدم مع التالى، فح لا يصح الحكم بالعناد على جميع التقادير و الالزم معاندة الشيء لنفسه و ان شئت فقل: ان المقدم اذا كان مع التالي، يجب ان يكون معانداً لنقيض التالي ضرورة امتناع اجتماع النقيضين فلوكان التالى معانداً للمقدم لزم معاندة الشيء للنقيضين، هذا. و لعل المصنف اشار الى هذه الدقيقة حيث اضاف التقادير إلى المقدم بطريق الاضافة العهدية فتأمل. (ميرزا محمد على)
On the divisions of the conditional: essential of the two parts and the accidental. Then the judgment in the conditional if it is upon all possible conditions of the antecedent (ه) such as 'this thing is either a stone or a tree' (17) and the disjunctive which prevents emptiness (18) is that in which it is judged with mutual incompatibility of the two relations that they only combine in falsehood, like your saying 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown' (19). His saying 'or only truth' means not in falsehood or disregarding falsehood (20) such that the two relations may or may not combine in falsehood, and the first meaning is called 'preventing combination in the specific sense' (21) and the second 'preventing combination in the general sense'. His saying 'or only falsehood' means not in truth or disregarding truth, and the first is 'preventing emptiness in the specific sense' and the second 'preventing emptiness in the general sense'. (ه) i.e. if the judgment of implication and opposition in the conditional is upon all possible states and conditions that can combine with the antecedent, even if they are impossible in themselves, then the proposition is universal, like our saying 'Whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists' and our saying 'This number is either even or odd' for the judgment of implication in the first and opposition in the second is upon all possible states and conditions, meaning that the existence of daytime is necessary for sunrise in all its states, times, and conditions, whether it is eclipsed or not, obscured by clouds or not, in Aries or Cancer or other, and with Zayd's speech or 'Amr's hitting etc., which cannot be counted, and similarly the opposition of evenness and oddness. Then we only considered the possibility of combination because if [the condition] were absolute, no universal conditional would be true, neither conjunctive nor disjunctive. As for the first, because among the conditions there is what does not entail the consequent, like the antecedent with the absence of the consequent, so the judgment of entailment upon all conditions of the antecedent would not be valid, and it would necessitate the conjunction of opposites. And as for the second, because among the conditions there is what does not oppose the consequent with the antecedent, like the antecedent with the consequent, so the judgment of opposition upon all conditions would not be valid, and it would necessitate a thing opposing itself. And if you wish, say: when the antecedent is with the consequent, it must oppose the negation of the consequent, necessarily impossible to combine opposites; so if the consequent opposed the antecedent, it would necessitate a thing opposing both opposites, that's it. And perhaps the author indicated this subtlety when he added 'conditions' to 'the antecedent' by way of an essential addition, so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines and subdivides the disjunctive conditional. It begins by stating that conditionals are divided into two types based on the relation of their parts: essential (dhati) and accidental (ittifaqi). Then it explains the judgment in conditionals: if the judgment applies to all possible conditions of the antecedent, it is universal, like 'this thing is either a stone or a tree'. Then it defines the disjunctive that prevents emptiness, which is one where the two sides are mutually exclusive only in falsehood, exemplified by 'Zayd is either in the sea or he does not drown'. The commentary distinguishes between 'preventing combination in the specific sense' (both sides cannot be true) and 'preventing combination in the general sense' (they may both be false). Similarly, 'preventing emptiness' has a specific sense (both cannot be false) and a general sense (they may both be true). The footnote (ه) clarifies that for a universal conditional, the judgment must hold for all possible conditions that can combine with the antecedent, even if those conditions are impossible in themselves. It gives examples: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists' (all possible conditions of sunrise entail daytime) and 'This number is either even or odd' (opposition holds in all conditions). The note then explains why the possibility of combination is considered: if it were not, universal conditionals would be false because they would require impossible conditions like the antecedent with the absence of the consequent (which would contradict the judgment) or the antecedent with the consequent (which would make the consequent oppose itself). This subtlety is indicated by the author's phrasing 'conditions of the antecedent' with an essential addition.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
شرطية كلية

A) Man'at al-jam' bi al-ma'na al-akhass
B) Man'at al-khuluw bi al-ma'na al-akhass
C) Man'at al-jam' bi al-ma'na al-a'am
D) Man'at al-khuluw bi al-ma'na al-a'am

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals are divided into man'at al-jam' (prevents joint truth) and man'at al-khuluw (prevents joint falsehood), each with specific and general senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Construct a disjunctive conditional related to a theological debate (e.g., 'The world is either created or eternal') and classify it as man'at al-jam' or man'at al-khuluw, specifying the sense.

The Condition for Universal Conditionals
Explains the condition for universal conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why universal conditionals require the judgment to hold for all possible conditions that can combine with the antecedent, and apply this to examples from kalam and fiqh.

Core Idea
A universal conditional must be true for every situation where the antecedent can occur, not for impossible combinations.

This lesson dives into the footnote (ه) which explains a crucial subtlety about universal conditionals. The author emphasizes that the judgment must apply to all possible states that can be combined with the antecedent. This ensures that universal conditionals are not falsified by impossible conditions. We will see how this applies to both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement for universality
The author says that for a conditional to be universal, the judgment (implication or opposition) must hold 'upon all possible conditions that can combine with the antecedent'. This means we only consider conditions that could actually occur alongside the antecedent, not impossible ones.
The Arabic phrase is: جميع التقادير والأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم
2
Analyze the given examples
The examples are 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists' (conjunctive) and 'This number is either even or odd' (disjunctive). In the first, the implication holds for every possible state of the sun shining (eclipsed, not eclipsed, etc.). In the second, the opposition holds for every possible number.
Note that 'impossible conditions' like the sun shining and daytime not existing are not considered because they cannot combine with the antecedent.
3
Understand why impossible combinations are excluded
If we were to consider impossible conditions, the conditional would be false. For example, if we considered the condition 'the sun shines and daytime does not exist', that combination is impossible, but if we required the conditional to be true for it, it would be false because daytime does not exist when the sun shines? Actually, the example shows that if we considered the antecedent with the absence of the consequent, that condition is impossible because the antecedent implies the consequent. So we only consider conditions that are possible combinations.
The text states that if we didn't restrict to possible combinations, it would lead to contradictions like a thing opposing itself.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the universal conditional: 'Whenever one performs wudu, one is in a state of ritual purity.' This must hold for all possible conditions of performing wudu: with running water, with rainwater, with full intention, etc. But what about the condition 'performing wudu while already impure'? That is still part of the antecedent. The judgment holds.
Ensure that the antecedent 'performing wudu' includes all its possible states.

Essential vs Accidental Opposition in Disjunctives
Distinguishes essential from accidental opposition and names resulting disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhātiyya) and accidental (ittifāqiyya) opposition in disjunctive conditionals and identify the resulting types of disjunctives.

Core Idea
Essential opposition is intrinsic; accidental opposition depends on context.

This passage explains the crucial distinction between two types of opposition in disjunctive conditionals: essential and accidental. Understanding this distinction is key to classifying disjunctives and evaluating their logical force. The commentator uses the example of evenness vs oddness for essential opposition, and blackness vs writing in a man for accidental opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the disjunctive
Locate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) in the disjunction. These are the two parts that are claimed to be incompatible.
In a disjunctive conditional, the opposition is between these two parts.
2
Determine the source of opposition
Ask: Is the incompatibility due to the very natures (dhāt) of the two parts, regardless of where they occur? If yes, the opposition is essential (dhātiyya). If the incompatibility only appears in a particular subject or matter, it is accidental (ittifāqiyya).
Essential opposition holds in every instance; accidental may not.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
Evenness and oddness are opposites by nature; in any number, they cannot coexist. Blackness and writing can coexist in a person (e.g., a black writer), so their opposition is accidental.
The example in the passage assumes a human subject.
4
Classify the disjunctive
If the opposition is essential, the disjunctive is called 'adversarial' (ʻinādiyya). If accidental, it is called 'coincidental' (ittifāqiyya). The commentator notes that if the disjunctive is 'absolute' (mutlaqa) – not explicitly qualified – it falls under the coincidental type.
This classification affects the logical rules that apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «لذاتي الجزئين» اى ان كان المنافاة بين الطرفين اي المقدم والتالى منافاة ناشئة عن ذاتيهما (۲۲) فى اى مادة تحققا كالمنافاة بين الزوجية والفردية (۲۳) لا عن خصوص المادة (۲۴) كالمنافاة بين السواد والكتابة (۲۵) في انسان يكون اسود و [؟] في انسان يكون [؟]. التنافى بكونه ذاتياً، سميت منفصلة عنادية و ان قيد بالاتفاق سميت منفصلة اتفاقية هذا. وعلى ما اختاره المصنف يندرج المطلقة تحت الاتفاقية فافهم. (محمد على)
His saying 'essential of the two parts' means: if the opposition between the two sides — i.e., the antecedent and consequent — is an opposition arising from their essences (22) in whatever matter they occur, like the opposition between evenness and oddness (23), not from the particular matter (24), like the opposition between blackness and writing (25) in a man who is black and a writer? etc. When the opposition is essential, it is called 'disjunctive of opposition'; and if it is conditioned by coincidence, it is called 'disjunctive of coincidence.' This. And according to what the author chose, the absolute [disjunctive] falls under the coincidental one, so understand. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'essential of the two parts' from the previous division. It defines essential opposition as one that arises from the very essences of the two terms, regardless of the particular matter in which they occur, like the opposition between evenness and oddness—these concepts are inherently incompatible wherever they are found. In contrast, accidental opposition arises from a particular material context, such as the opposition between blackness and writing in a man who is both black and a writer; this opposition is not inherent to blackness and writing themselves, because they can coexist in other subjects. When opposition is essential, the disjunctive is called 'adversarial' (ʻinādiyya); when it is coincidental (ittifāqiyya). The commentator notes that according to the author, the 'absolute' disjunctive (which is not explicitly quantified) falls under the coincidental type. This is a subtle point: if the disjunction is not specified as essential, it is treated as accidental, because essential opposition must be explicit.

Key Terms
منافاة ذاتية
منافاة اتفاقية
منفصلة عنادية
منفصلة اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of essential opposition as defined in the passage?
A) Opposition between blackness and writing in a man
B) Opposition between evenness and oddness
C) Opposition between standing and sitting in Zayd
D) Opposition between heat and cold in a particular room

Key Takeaway
Essential opposition arises from the essences of the parts, while accidental opposition depends on the particular matter; this determines whether the disjunctive is called ʻinādiyya or ittifāqiyya.
